Compound Ignorance: A Psychic Disease
Author defines compound ignorance as a psychic disease and compares it to bodily illness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of compound ignorance and distinguish it from simple ignorance using the analogy with bodily health.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is not just not knowing—it is actively believing what is false.

This passage defines compound ignorance as a psychic disease and compares it to bodily illness. Understanding this distinction is crucial for identifying epistemic errors in Islamic sciences, where a person may hold false beliefs while lacking true knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of compound ignorance
Find the phrase 'ومن الجهل ما هو مركب وهو جهل على سبيل الغنية والملكة وإنه مرض نفساني' and note that it is described as a disposition (malaka) and a psychic disease (marad nafsani).
2
Identify the two deficits
The author explicitly states: 'فيه خلاف العلم من وجهين: أحدهما خلو النفس عن العلم والثاني أن يحذف ضد العلم مع ذلك الخلو'. Extract both: (1) emptiness of knowledge, (2) presence of the opposite of knowledge (false belief).
3
Understand the analogy with bodily health
Read the comparison: 'كما أن البدن إذا أحدث فيه أمر غريب على خلاف مزاجه الأصلي صار مريضًا فكذلك النفس إذا اعتقدت الآراء الباطلة...' Recognize that just as a bodily foreign element disrupts original temper, false beliefs disrupt the soul's original sound nature (fitra).
The original psychic health is the disposition for true sciences.
4
Recognize how compound ignorance arises
The passage mentions it may occur 'ابتداءً' (spontaneously) or 'باكساب قياس شيء' (through acquiring a syllogism). This introduces the role of faulty reasoning in generating compound ignorance.
This leads to the study of deceptive syllogisms in the five arts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النفس عن الرأي بل الحق أن هذا الجهل لا يكتسب. ومن الجهل ما هو مركب وهو جهل على سبيل الغنية والملكة وإنه مرض نفساني. وذلك لأن صحة كل شيء إما تكون موجودة بفطرته الأصلية أو بالكسب؛ وذلك كمالا تاما فيكون على وجهين في البدن بالنسبة إلى مزاجه الأصلي وغيره؛ وفي النفس كذلك. والصحة الثابتة لها هي أن تحصل الزوائد الكمالية التي تستعد بتلك الصحة المزاجية الأصلية وهو العلوم الحقيقية. وكما أن البدن إذا أحدث فيه أمر غريب على خلاف مزاجه الأصلي صار مريضًا فكذلك النفس إذا اعتقدت الآراء الباطلة المخالفة لما يكون مبنياً على فطرتها الأصلية صارت مريضة. وإنما يسمى هذا الجهل مركَّبًا لأن فيه خلاف العلم من وجهين: أحدهما خلو النفس عن العلم والثاني أن يحذف ضد العلم مع ذلك الخلو. وهذا النوع من الجهل قد يقع ابتداءً وإذعانًا للنفس له، وقد يقع باكتساب قياس شيء؛ وإنه إما أن يكون فيما لا وسط له، أو فيما له وسط. وهذا إما أن يكون الأوسط فيه من الأشياء المناسبة أو من الأشياء القريبة وجميع ذلك إما أن يكون الأوسط فيه هو أوسط القياس الصادق بعينه، أو ليس ولا يخلو من أن يكون مقابله حقا سالبا فيكون هو موجبا؛ ويقع في الشكل الأول فقط إن كان كليا، أو يكون مقابله حقا موجبًا فيكون هو سالبا؛ وإنه يقع في الشكل الأول والثاني معا إن كان كليا.
The soul regarding opinion, but the truth is that this ignorance is not acquired. And of ignorance there is compound ignorance, which is ignorance as a disposition and habit, and it is a psychic disease. That is because the health of everything either exists by its original nature or by acquisition; that is a perfect perfection, so it is in two aspects in the body regarding its original temperament and other than it; and likewise in the soul. The established health of it is that it attains the perfective increments prepared by that original temperamental health, which are the true sciences. Just as when a foreign matter contrary to its original temperament occurs in the body it becomes sick, so too when the soul believes false opinions contrary to what is based on its original nature it becomes sick. This ignorance is only called ‘compound’ because in it there is opposition to knowledge in two respects: one is the soul’s emptiness of knowledge, and the second is that the opposite of knowledge is attached with that emptiness. This type of ignorance may occur initially and through the soul’s assent to it, and it may occur through acquiring a syllogism; and it either is in what has no middle term, or in what has a middle term. And this either has its middle term from appropriate things or from proximate things, and all that either has its middle term identical to the middle term of a true syllogism, or not, and it cannot but be that its opposite is a true negative so it is affirmative, and it occurs only in the first figure if it is universal; or its opposite is a true affirmative so it is negative, and it occurs in both the first and second figures if it is universal.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage defines compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) and draws an analogy with bodily health. The author begins by stating that some ignorance is not acquired (perhaps innate), then introduces compound ignorance as a habitual state that is a psychic disease. He explains that health, whether bodily or psychic, can be original or acquired; for the soul, original health is the disposition for true sciences. When the soul adopts false opinions contrary to its innate sound disposition, it becomes diseased, just as a body becomes ill when a foreign element disrupts its original temperament. The term 'compound' is justified because this ignorance involves two deficits: the absence of knowledge and the presence of a false belief contrary to knowledge. The author then categorizes how compound ignorance arises—either spontaneously or via a syllogistic inference. The syllogism that produces it may involve a middle term that is either immediate or mediated, and that middle term may be appropriate or proximate, and may or may not match the middle term of a true syllogism. The resulting false conclusion, when universal, occurs in the first figure if the true proposition is a universal negative, or in both first and second figures if the true proposition is a universal affirmative. This analysis sets the stage for a detailed study of deceptive syllogisms. (Note: The phrase 'via a syllogism of a thing' suggests that compound ignorance can result from faulty reasoning, which will be elaborated in the next passage.)

Key Terms
الجهل المركب
الصحة النفسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two conditions constitute compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab)?
A) Absence of knowledge and presence of true belief
B) Presence of knowledge and absence of false belief
C) Absence of knowledge and presence of false belief
D) Presence of doubt and absence of certainty

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is a psychic disease involving both ignorance and false belief, often resulting from faulty reasoning, and it must be distinguished from simple ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a historical example of compound ignorance in Islamic theology: e.g., the Mu'tazila who denied the beatific vision (false belief) while lacking true knowledge of the relevant hadiths. Explain which component is absent knowledge and which is false belief.
?
Contrast compound ignorance with simple ignorance: a layperson who has never heard of the prohibition of riba. Which type of ignorance do they have? Why?

The Four Causes as Middle Terms
Author classifies the four causes and their attributes as middle terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four causes (formal, efficient, final, material) is used as the middle term in a given demonstrative why-question from an Islamic science text.

Core Idea
Every cause can be the middle term in a demonstration answering 'why'.

In this passage, the author explains that all four types of causes—formal, efficient, final, and material—can serve as the middle term in a logical demonstration that answers 'why' something is the case. Understanding which cause is being used is essential for analyzing arguments in Islamic sciences like kalam, fiqh, and medicine. We will walk through each cause with examples and then practice identifying them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'why' question
Find the phenomenon being questioned. The answer will provide the cause that serves as the middle term.
Example: 'Why does that thing move?'
2
Determine the type of cause in the answer
Classify the answer as one of the four causes: if it gives the form or essence, it's formal; if it gives the agent, it's efficient; if it gives the purpose, it's final; if it gives the material composition, it's material.
In Islamic theology, 'Why is the world created?' answered by 'Because Allah created it' uses efficient cause.
3
Verify it functions as a middle term
The cause should be a term that connects the subject and predicate of the conclusion, making the syllogism valid.
In the text, the formal cause example uses equality as the middle term.
4
Practice with an Islamic science example
Take a demonstration from kalam or fiqh and identify which cause is the middle term.
For example: 'Why is nabidh forbidden?' 'Because it is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' Here, intoxication is the formal cause (essence of nabidh making it forbidden).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كلها يصلح أن يوضع حدودًا وسطى في جواب «لِمَ»؛ وذلك لأن كل علة لشيء في شيء فهو واسطة بينهما مثلا إذا قلنا هذه الزاوية مساوية لتلك الزاوية لما أنها مساوية لما يساويها : فهذا من جملة ما وضع فيه الحد الأوسط علة صورية. وقد توضع العلة الفاعلية كما إذا قلتَ لِمَ يتحرك ذلك الشيء فيقال لأنه يحركه فلان وقد توضع العلة الغائية كذلك كما إذا قلت لم يمشي فلان فيقال لكي يصح؛ فكأنه يقول فلان يطلب أن يصح، ومن يطلب أن يصح يمشي للرياضة؛ فالحد الأوسط هو من الغائية. وقد توضع العلة المادية أيضًا كما إذا قلت لم يفسد ذلك الشيء فيقال لأنه مركب من أمور متضادة. ثم كل واحد من هذه العلل قد تكون قريبة وهي التي لا يكون بينها وبين المعلول واسطة، وقد تكون بعيدة وهي التي يكون بينها وبين المعلول واسطة وقد تكون بالذات كشُرب السقمونيا لإسهال الصفراء، وقد تكون بالعرض كشر به لتبريد المزاج، وقد تكون بالفعل وإنه سبب لكون المعلول بالفعل وقد تكون بالقوة وإنها لا تكون سببًا لكون المعلول بالقوة لأن كون المعلول بالقوة لذات المعلول لا غيره. وهذه العلل إذا وجدت معا وجد المعلول لا محالة.
Then all of them (the causes) are suitable to be placed as middle terms in answering 'why?'; that is because every cause of something in something is a middle term between them. For example, when we say: 'This angle is equal to that angle because it is equal to what is equal to it' — this is among the cases where the middle term is a formal cause. And the efficient cause may be placed, as when you say: 'Why does that thing move?' It is said: 'Because so-and-so moves it.' And the final cause may be placed similarly, as when you say: 'Why did so-and-so walk?' It is said: 'In order to become healthy'; it is as if he says: 'So-and-so seeks to become healthy, and whoever seeks to become healthy walks for exercise'; so the middle term is from the final cause. And the material cause may also be placed, as when you say: 'Why did that thing corrupt?' It is said: 'Because it is composed of opposing things.' Then each one of these causes may be near — that which has no intermediary between it and the effect; and may be far — that which has an intermediary between it and the effect; and may be essential — like drinking scammony for expelling bile; and may be accidental — like drinking it for cooling the temperament; and may be actual — and it is a cause for the effect being actual; and may be potential — and it is not a cause for the effect being potential, because the effect's being potential belongs to the essence of the effect, not to something else. And these causes, when they exist together, the effect necessarily exists.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of causes and their attributes as they serve as middle terms in syllogisms. The author begins by stating that all causes can serve as middle terms in answering 'why' questions. He then enumerates the four types of causes: formal, efficient, final, and material, each with a concrete example. For the formal cause, he gives the example of angle equality; for efficient, a moving agent; for final, walking for health; for material, corruption due to opposing components. After listing the four, the author subdivides each cause into pairs: near vs. far (depending on whether an intermediary exists between cause and effect), essential vs. accidental (based on inherent vs. incidental relation), and actual vs. potential (whether the cause brings about the effect in actuality or potentiality). The crucial point is that potential causes do not cause potential effects, because potentiality is intrinsic to the effect itself. Finally, he states that when all causes for a given effect are simultaneously present, the effect necessarily follows. This establishes a foundational principle for causal reasoning in demonstration.

Key Terms
علة صورية
علة فاعلية
علة غائية
علة مادية
قريبة
بعيدة
بالذات
بالعرض
بالفعل
بالقوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of cause is exemplified by 'he walks in order to become healthy'?
A) Formal cause
B) Efficient cause
C) Final cause
D) Material cause

Key Takeaway
The four causes—formal, efficient, final, material—all can serve as middle terms in demonstrations answering 'why', and each must be correctly identified to analyze causal reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following statement from a fiqh text: 'The sale is valid because it includes offer and acceptance.' Identify which of the four causes is the middle term and explain why.
?
Construct a demonstrative argument using the final cause as the middle term: 'Why does a person perform tayammum?' Answer using a final cause, and then identify the middle term.

Refuting Two Misconceptions about Dialectical Syllogism
Author refutes two mistaken views and gives correct definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two common mistakes about dialectical syllogism and explain why each is corrupt using the author's arguments.

Core Idea
Dialectical syllogism is defined by accepted premises, not truth.

Some mistakenly think dialectical syllogisms have mostly true premises, while others think the dialectical art produces truth in most cases. Both views are wrong. In this passage, the author defines dialectical syllogism correctly: it is composed of premises that are accepted (musallama) or well-known (mashhura), regardless of their truth-value. Understanding this distinction is crucial for anyone engaging in dialectical debate in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two misconceptions
Look for the phrases 'قد ظن قوم أن...' and 'وغير ذلك القوم قوم ظنوا أن...' These introduce the two false views.
2
Extract the author's refutation of the first view
The author says the dialectical syllogism is composed of accepted or well-known premises, and truth is not required.
3
Extract the author's refutation of the second view
The author argues that an art is defined by necessary properties of its parts, not by accidental consequences like mostly producing truth.
4
Summarize the correct definition
The correct definition is given in the refutation: dialectical syllogism is a syllogism with accepted or well-known premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في إبانة ما غلط فيه من أمر القياس الجدلي وتعريف الموضع والمقدمة وأسباب الشهرة وإعطاء السبب في تسمية هذا الكتاب بالمواضع قد ظن قوم أن القياسات الجدلية هي التي موضوعاتها مقدمات أكثرية، وغير ذلك القوم قوم ظنوا أن الصناعة الجدلية هي التي تنتج الحق في أكثر الأمور؛ وهذه كلها ظنون فاسدة فإن القياس الجدلي إنما هو قياس جدلي بأن يكون مؤلفًا من مقدمات مسلمة أو مشهورة، وليس من شرط كونها مشهورة أو مسلمة أن تكون صادقة يمكن أن تكون مشهورة ومسلمة وهي غير صادقة. وأما أن الصناعة الجدلية فهي التي كذا فذلك تعريف الصناعة تعريفا فاسدا؛ إذ الصناعة تعرف بما يجب أن يكون موجودًا كل جزء من أجزائها؛ وإنتاجها في أكثر الأمور لا يكون كذلك. ثمَّ الشهرة لا يمتنع أن تكون موجودة للباطل كما للحق حتى تكون المقدمات المشهورة الحقة مساوية للمقدمات المشهورة الباطلة بل إن اتفق أن يغلب أحد الطرفين فإنما يتفق اتفاقا لا وجوبًا. فإذا كان اتفاقا فلا اعتماد عليه، ولا يمتنع أن تكون النتائج الحقة والباطلة يتفق لها أن يكون سواء. وإن كان لقائل إن الحقة غالبة فإن الباطلة لا تكون إلا عن الباطلة والحقة قد تكون من الحقة، وقد تكون من الباطلة. لكنا نقول هذا مسلم لكن يمكن أن يتفق أن يكون عدد ما ينتج الباطلة بالفعل أكثر من عدد ما ينتج الحقة والباطلة معًا بالفعل. وأما الجدلي فلا يجب عليه أن يستعمل بحال كيفية الصدق والكذب في المقدمة؛ ولا في النتيجة كذلك بل عليه أن يلتفت إلى أن تكون المقدمة مسلمة والنتيجة لازمة عنها. وقوم ظنوا أن الجدلي وإن كان له أن يتكلم في كل مسألة طبيعية وغير طبيعية فإنما يجب عليه أن لا يتكلم من مباديها بل من المبادئ المشهورة المشتركة؛ وليس كذلك بل يجب أن يقال إنه ليس يكون جدليا بأن يكون كلامه متصورًا على استعمال مبادئ تلك الصناعة بأعيانها كما يكون كلام التعليمي متصورًا عليه بل إن له أن يستعمل المبادئ المشتركة أيضًا الغريبة عن ذلك إذا كانت مشهورة على أن يعلم أن له أن يستعمل المبادئ الخاصة بتلك الصناعة المشهورة فيما بين أهلها ، فله أي للجدلي أن يستعمل الشيء من حيث هو مشهورة عند طائفة مثل كون الشمس أكبر من الأرض. وربَّما كان سببًا ليس عليه برهان مثل کون زخال نحس، والمشتري سعد لكن ليس له أن يستعمل من أحكام صناعة ومباديها إذا لم تكن مشهورة إلا على سبيل التسلم من خصمه حين يسلم ما هو غير مشهورة.
Chapter: On clarifying the mistake regarding dialectical syllogism, defining the topic and premise, the causes of notoriety, and giving the reason for naming this book 'Topics'. Some people thought that dialectical syllogisms are those whose subject matters are premises that are mostly true, and others thought that the dialectical art is that which produces truth in most matters; all these are corrupt opinions. For the dialectical syllogism is only a dialectical syllogism in that it is composed of accepted or well-known premises, and it is not a condition for them being well-known or accepted that they be true; it is possible for them to be well-known and accepted while being false. As for saying that the dialectical art is such and such, that is a corrupt definition of the art; for the art is defined by what must exist in every part of it; and its producing truth in most matters is not like that. Moreover, notoriety is not prevented from existing for falsehood as for truth, such that true well-known premises are equal to false well-known premises; rather, if one side happens to prevail, it happens by chance, not necessarily. So if it is by chance, then there is no reliance on it, and it is not impossible that true and false conclusions happen to be equal. And if someone says that true ones are predominant, because false ones only come from false premises, while true ones can come from true or false premises, we say: this is granted, but it can happen that the number of what is actually produced from false premises is greater than the number of what is actually produced from both true and false together. As for the dialectician, he is not required at all to use the quality of truth and falsehood in the premise; nor in the conclusion likewise; rather, he must pay attention to the premise being accepted and the conclusion following from it. And some people thought that the dialectician, although he can speak about every natural and non-natural issue, must not speak from its principles but from the common well-known principles; it is not so; rather, it must be said that he is not a dialectician if his speech is conceived as using the very principles of that art, as the speech of the demonstrative teacher is conceived. Rather, he can also use the common principles that are foreign to it, if they are well-known, while knowing that he can use the specific principles of that art that are well-known among its practitioners. So the dialectician can use a thing by virtue of it being well-known among a group, such as the sun being larger than the earth. And sometimes there is a cause for which there is no demonstration, such as Saturn being ill-omened and Jupiter being auspicious. But he cannot use the rulings and principles of an art if they are not well-known, except by way of acceptance from his opponent when the latter accepts what is not well-known.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of two mistaken views about dialectical syllogism, followed by the correct definition and further clarifications. The author begins by stating the purpose of the chapter: to clarify the mistake regarding dialectical syllogism, define the topic (al-mawḍiʿ) and premise (al-muqaddima), the causes of notoriety (shuhra), and the reason for naming this book 'Topics'. Then he presents two erroneous views: first, that dialectical syllogisms are those whose premises are mostly true; second, that the dialectical art is that which produces truth in most matters. He declares both as corrupt. The correct definition is that a dialectical syllogism is composed of premises that are either accepted (musallama) or well-known (mashhūra). Importantly, it is not a condition that such premises be true; they can be false yet still be well-known or accepted. He then criticizes the definition of the art itself: an art is defined by what must be present in every part of it, not by an accidental property like producing truth in most cases. Notoriety can attach to falsehood as much as to truth; if one side prevails, it is by chance, not necessity. Therefore, no reliance can be placed on that. He addresses a possible objection that true conclusions are more numerous, responding that even if true conclusions can come from false premises, it is still possible that the count of false conclusions actually exceeds that of true ones. Then he turns to the dialectician's task: he is not concerned with the truth or falsehood of premises, only with them being accepted and the conclusion necessarily following. Another mistaken view is that the dialectician must only use common principles, not the specific principles of the art. The author corrects this: the dialectician can use the specific principles of an art if they are well-known among its practitioners, and he can also use common principles. However, if a principle of an art is not well-known, he can only use it if the opponent accepts it. He gives examples: the sun being larger than the earth is well-known, and there is no demonstration for Saturn being ill-omened or Jupiter auspicious, but they can be used if accepted. This passage clarifies the nature of dialectic: it is based on accepted premises, not on demonstration's requirement of truth.

Key Terms
القياس الجدلي (al-qiyās al-jadalī): A syllogism composed of premises that are accepted (musallama) or well-known (mashhūra), regardless of their truth value. [Note: Stable term in Aristotelian-Islamic logic; here opposed to demonstrative syllogism.]
مسلمة (musallama): A premise that is granted or accepted by the interlocutor in a dialectical exchange. [Note: No significant shift.]
مشهورة (mashhūra): A premise that is well-known or widely accepted, even if false. [Note: No significant shift.]
الصناعة الجدلية (al-ṣināʿa al-jadaliyya): The dialectical art; defined by necessary properties of its constituent parts, not by accidental production of truth. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct definition of a dialectical syllogism?
A) A syllogism that produces truth in most matters.
B) A syllogism composed of premises that are true.
C) A syllogism composed of accepted or well-known premises.
D) A syllogism whose premises are mostly true.

Key Takeaway
Dialectical syllogism is defined by its premises being accepted or well-known, not by their truth-value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the following premises can be used in a dialectical syllogism according to the passage: a) 'All humans are mortal' (demonstrative), b) 'The earth is flat' (well-known but false), c) 'God is just' (accepted). Explain your reasoning.
?
Construct a dialectical syllogism on a fiqh issue (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is haram') and explain why it counts as dialectical if the premises are accepted, even if the opponent does not accept them.

The Dialectician's Use of Premises and Notoriety
Author clarifies the role of notoriety and the dialectician's use of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the dialectician is not concerned with truth and how he can use premises from specific arts if they are well-known.

Core Idea
The dialectician cares only about acceptance, not truth; he can use any well-known premise, even from specific disciplines.

The author continues to clarify the nature of dialectic. He explains that notoriety (shuhra) can attach to falsehoods as well as truths, and that the dialectician's only requirement is that premises be accepted and the conclusion follow. He also corrects the misconception that the dialectician must restrict himself to common principles; in fact, he can use the well-known principles of any art. This lesson examines these important clarifications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the relationship between notoriety and truth
The author says notoriety exists for falsehood as for truth; prevalence is by chance, not necessity.
2
Grasp the dialectician's focus
He must ensure the premise is accepted and the conclusion follows; truth is irrelevant.
3
Identify the third misconception
Some thought the dialectician must only use common principles.
4
Analyze the correct view
The dialectician can use the well-known principles of a specific art, and also common principles; he can use accepted premises even without demonstration.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في إبانة ما غلط فيه من أمر القياس الجدلي وتعريف الموضع والمقدمة وأسباب الشهرة وإعطاء السبب في تسمية هذا الكتاب بالمواضع قد ظن قوم أن القياسات الجدلية هي التي موضوعاتها مقدمات أكثرية، وغير ذلك القوم قوم ظنوا أن الصناعة الجدلية هي التي تنتج الحق في أكثر الأمور؛ وهذه كلها ظنون فاسدة فإن القياس الجدلي إنما هو قياس جدلي بأن يكون مؤلفًا من مقدمات مسلمة أو مشهورة، وليس من شرط كونها مشهورة أو مسلمة أن تكون صادقة يمكن أن تكون مشهورة ومسلمة وهي غير صادقة. وأما أن الصناعة الجدلية فهي التي كذا فذلك تعريف الصناعة تعريفا فاسدا؛ إذ الصناعة تعرف بما يجب أن يكون موجودًا كل جزء من أجزائها؛ وإنتاجها في أكثر الأمور لا يكون كذلك. ثمَّ الشهرة لا يمتنع أن تكون موجودة للباطل كما للحق حتى تكون المقدمات المشهورة الحقة مساوية للمقدمات المشهورة الباطلة بل إن اتفق أن يغلب أحد الطرفين فإنما يتفق اتفاقا لا وجوبًا. فإذا كان اتفاقا فلا اعتماد عليه، ولا يمتنع أن تكون النتائج الحقة والباطلة يتفق لها أن يكون سواء. وإن كان لقائل إن الحقة غالبة فإن الباطلة لا تكون إلا عن الباطلة والحقة قد تكون من الحقة، وقد تكون من الباطلة. لكنا نقول هذا مسلم لكن يمكن أن يتفق أن يكون عدد ما ينتج الباطلة بالفعل أكثر من عدد ما ينتج الحقة والباطلة معًا بالفعل. وأما الجدلي فلا يجب عليه أن يستعمل بحال كيفية الصدق والكذب في المقدمة؛ ولا في النتيجة كذلك بل عليه أن يلتفت إلى أن تكون المقدمة مسلمة والنتيجة لازمة عنها. وقوم ظنوا أن الجدلي وإن كان له أن يتكلم في كل مسألة طبيعية وغير طبيعية فإنما يجب عليه أن لا يتكلم من مباديها بل من المبادئ المشهورة المشتركة؛ وليس كذلك بل يجب أن يقال إنه ليس يكون جدليا بأن يكون كلامه متصورًا على استعمال مبادئ تلك الصناعة بأعيانها كما يكون كلام التعليمي متصورًا عليه بل إن له أن يستعمل المبادئ المشتركة أيضًا الغريبة عن ذلك إذا كانت مشهورة على أن يعلم أن له أن يستعمل المبادئ الخاصة بتلك الصناعة المشهورة فيما بين أهلها ، فله أي للجدلي أن يستعمل الشيء من حيث هو مشهورة عند طائفة مثل كون الشمس أكبر من الأرض. وربَّما كان سببًا ليس عليه برهان مثل کون زخال نحس، والمشتري سعد لكن ليس له أن يستعمل من أحكام صناعة ومباديها إذا لم تكن مشهورة إلا على سبيل التسلم من خصمه حين يسلم ما هو غير مشهورة.
Chapter: On clarifying the mistake regarding dialectical syllogism, defining the topic and premise, the causes of notoriety, and giving the reason for naming this book 'Topics'. Some people thought that dialectical syllogisms are those whose subject matters are premises that are mostly true, and others thought that the dialectical art is that which produces truth in most matters; all these are corrupt opinions. For the dialectical syllogism is only a dialectical syllogism in that it is composed of accepted or well-known premises, and it is not a condition for them being well-known or accepted that they be true; it is possible for them to be well-known and accepted while being false. As for saying that the dialectical art is such and such, that is a corrupt definition of the art; for the art is defined by what must exist in every part of it; and its producing truth in most matters is not like that. Moreover, notoriety is not prevented from existing for falsehood as for truth, such that true well-known premises are equal to false well-known premises; rather, if one side happens to prevail, it happens by chance, not necessarily. So if it is by chance, then there is no reliance on it, and it is not impossible that true and false conclusions happen to be equal. And if someone says that true ones are predominant, because false ones only come from false premises, while true ones can come from true or false premises, we say: this is granted, but it can happen that the number of what is actually produced from false premises is greater than the number of what is actually produced from both true and false together. As for the dialectician, he is not required at all to use the quality of truth and falsehood in the premise; nor in the conclusion likewise; rather, he must pay attention to the premise being accepted and the conclusion following from it. And some people thought that the dialectician, although he can speak about every natural and non-natural issue, must not speak from its principles but from the common well-known principles; it is not so; rather, it must be said that he is not a dialectician if his speech is conceived as using the very principles of that art, as the speech of the demonstrative teacher is conceived. Rather, he can also use the common principles that are foreign to it, if they are well-known, while knowing that he can use the specific principles of that art that are well-known among its practitioners. So the dialectician can use a thing by virtue of it being well-known among a group, such as the sun being larger than the earth. And sometimes there is a cause for which there is no demonstration, such as Saturn being ill-omened and Jupiter being auspicious. But he cannot use the rulings and principles of an art if they are not well-known, except by way of acceptance from his opponent when the latter accepts what is not well-known.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of two mistaken views about dialectical syllogism, followed by the correct definition and further clarifications. The author begins by stating the purpose of the chapter: to clarify the mistake regarding dialectical syllogism, define the topic (al-mawḍiʿ) and premise (al-muqaddima), the causes of notoriety (shuhra), and the reason for naming this book 'Topics'. Then he presents two erroneous views: first, that dialectical syllogisms are those whose premises are mostly true; second, that the dialectical art is that which produces truth in most matters. He declares both as corrupt. The correct definition is that a dialectical syllogism is composed of premises that are either accepted (musallama) or well-known (mashhūra). Importantly, it is not a condition that such premises be true; they can be false yet still be well-known or accepted. He then criticizes the definition of the art itself: an art is defined by what must be present in every part of it, not by an accidental property like producing truth in most cases. Notoriety can attach to falsehood as much as to truth; if one side prevails, it is by chance, not necessity. Therefore, no reliance can be placed on that. He addresses a possible objection that true conclusions are more numerous, responding that even if true conclusions can come from false premises, it is still possible that the count of false conclusions actually exceeds that of true ones. Then he turns to the dialectician's task: he is not concerned with the truth or falsehood of premises, only with them being accepted and the conclusion necessarily following. Another mistaken view is that the dialectician must only use common principles, not the specific principles of the art. The author corrects this: the dialectician can use the specific principles of an art if they are well-known among its practitioners, and he can also use common principles. However, if a principle of an art is not well-known, he can only use it if the opponent accepts it. He gives examples: the sun being larger than the earth is well-known, and there is no demonstration for Saturn being ill-omened or Jupiter auspicious, but they can be used if accepted. This passage clarifies the nature of dialectic: it is based on accepted premises, not on demonstration's requirement of truth.

Key Terms
شهرة (shuhra): Notoriety or widespread acceptance; can attach to falsehood as well as truth. [Note: No significant shift.]
مشهورة (mashhūra): A premise that is well-known or widely accepted, even if false. [Note: No significant shift.]
مسلمة (musallama): A premise that is granted or accepted by the interlocutor in a dialectical exchange. [Note: No significant shift.]
الجدلي (al-jadalī): The dialectician; one who uses dialectical syllogisms composed of accepted or well-known premises. [Note: No significant shift.]

The dialectician may use any premise that is well-known or accepted, including those specific to a field, without requiring demonstration.

What is a Dialectical Topic (al-Mawḍiʿ)?
Author defines 'topic' and warns against misuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a dialectical topic and distinguish between using it as a source of premises versus using it as a direct universal premise in an argument.

Core Idea
A topic is a reservoir of premises, not a premise itself.

In this lesson, we learn the definition of a 'topic' (al-mawḍiʿ) as a single principle from which many rulings can be derived for use in dialectical syllogisms. The author gives an example of a topic rule about contraries, but warns against using the rule directly as a premise because it may lead to confusion. This distinction is crucial for correctly applying the art of dialectic (jadal) in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of mawḍiʿ
Find the phrase in the Arabic that explains what a mawḍiʿ is. The text says: 'معنى الموضع منفرد من شأنه أن يُسْتَعْتَب منه أحكام كثيرة يجعل كل واحد منها جزء قياس'. This tells you that a topic is a single source from which many rulings are taken, each becoming a part of a syllogism.
Notice the word 'منفرد' (single) emphasizes it is one principle.
2
Identify the example topic rule
The author gives an example: 'إذا كان الضد موجودًا لشيء فضده سيكون موجودًا لضد الشيء' (If a contrary exists for a thing, then its contrary will exist for the contrary of that thing). This is a well-known rule that can generate many specific premises.
This rule is a general pattern, not a concrete premise.
3
Understand the author's caution
The author warns that using the rule directly in its universal form as a syllogistic premise can be problematic, because it may suggest something that doesn't hold. He gives the example of whiteness and blackness: both are contraries of something else, but the rule might be misapplied if taken as a blanket statement.
The phrase 'يشعر بما يفقده' means 'it suggests what it lacks' – the rule seems complete but may need refinement.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a dialectical argument, determine whether a premise is taken from a topic or used as a topic rule itself. If the premise is a general rule like 'every contrary has a contrary', it is the topic; if it is a specific instance like 'whiteness is contrary to blackness', it is a premise derived from the topic.
The art of dialectic uses topics to generate relevant premises, not to supply the entire reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكتاب الموضوع للجدل في التعليم الأول قد يسمى بكتاب الموضع؛ ومعنى الموضع منفرد من شأنه أن يُسْتَعْتَب منه أحكام كثيرة يجعل كل واحد منها جزء قياس مثل قول القائل إن كان الضد موجودًا لشيء فضده سيكون موجودًا لضد الشيء، فهذا حكم مشهور (۱)؛ وليس عنائه أن يستعمل في القياس على هذه الصفة. فإنه إذا استعمل بهذه الصفة فربَّما كان يصير لما أنه يشعر بما يفقده؛ إذ ربَّما يشعر عند تأمله بضد يكون لشيء ومقابلة ليس لضد الشيء بل بمسألة كالبياض فإنه للجسم؛ وأيضًا فإن السواد له. وأما إذا استعمل جزءه
And the book composed for dialectic in the First Education is called the Book of Topics. The meaning of 'topic' is a single [principle] from which many rulings are taken, each of which is made a part of a syllogism, such as one's saying: 'If a contrary exists for a thing, then its contrary will exist for the contrary of that thing.' This is a well-known ruling. But his concern is not to use it in a syllogism in this form. For if it is used in this form, it may become [problematic] because it suggests what it lacks; for when contemplating it, one might think of a contrary that exists for a thing and its opposite not being for the contrary of that thing, but rather in a matter like whiteness which is for the body; and also blackness is for it. But if he uses its part...
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage defines the 'topic' (al-mawḍiʿ) and introduces the book of Topics. The author states that the book written for dialectic in the First Education (i.e., Aristotle's Topics) is called the Book of Topics. Then he defines a topic: it is a single locus or principle from which many rulings (aḥkām) are derived, each ruling serving as a part of a syllogism. He gives an example: 'If a contrary exists for a thing, then its contrary will exist for the contrary of that thing.' This is a well-known rule. However, the author warns that one should not use this rule directly in its universal form as a syllogistic premise, because it may lead to confusion. For instance, when contemplating it, one might think of a case where a contrary exists for a thing but the opposite does not hold for the contrary, like whiteness and blackness: both are contraries and both apply to the body, but the rule as stated might be misapplied. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is that the topic should be used as a source for specific premises, not as a blanket rule.

جزء قياس (juz' qiyās)

Rhetorical Syllogisms: Definition, Audience, and Purpose
Author defines rhetorical syllogisms and distinguishes them from demonstration and dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define rhetorical syllogisms and distinguish them from demonstration and dialectic based on their purpose and target audience.

Core Idea
Rhetoric persuades the public, demonstration proves to the elite, and dialectic aims at victory.

This passage introduces the third type of syllogism: rhetorical syllogisms (al-khiṭābiyya). The author explains that while demonstration is for the elite and dialectic for debate, rhetoric serves to persuade the common people about what they should believe. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for choosing the right argumentative tool in Islamic scholarly discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined: 'rhetorical syllogisms' (al-qiāsāt al-khiṭābiyya). It is introduced as the third type of syllogism.
2
Extract the purpose
Read that rhetorical syllogisms aim 'toward persuasion of the public' (ilā iqnā‘ al-jumhūr). Note that the benefit returns to wisdom or dialectic, indicating their instrumental role.
3
Identify the audience categories
The author divides humanity into two groups: the elite (khāṣṣī) and the common (‘āmmī). The elite requires demonstration (burhān) for belief; the common is satisfied with rhetoric.
4
Distinguish from demonstration and dialectic
Demonstration produces certain truth for the elite; dialectic seeks victory/compulsion (ghalaba wa-ilzām) using accepted premises. Rhetoric is the only suitable art for the common.
Dialectic is not aimed at truth—the sophist seeks victory without producing belief.
5
Analyze the nature of belief for each audience
The elite's belief must be true and free from doubt; the common's belief does not require elimination of doubt. Rhetorical arguments may simply elicit assent ('you have spoken truthfully') without rigorous proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : في القياسات الخطابية. أما الخطابية فهي التي تعد نحو إقناع الجمهور فيما يحق عليهم أن يصدقوا به، ولينتفع عن نفع يعود منها على الحكمة أو على الجدل. ولما كان المخاطب إنسانًا، والإنسان إما خاصي وإما عامي. والخاصي لا ينتفع من حيث يحتاج أن يصدق تصديق الخواص إلا بالبرهان، والعامي لا ينتفع من حيث يحتاج أن يصدق تصديق العوام إلا بالخطابية؛ فالصناعة النافعة في أن يكتسب الإنسان تصديقا نافعا إما البرهان وإما الخطابة. وأما الجدل بالانتفاع بحصول الغرض وهو الغلبة والإلزام كما مر ؛ وليس قصد الغلبة هو بعينه قصد إفادة التصديق فإن السوفسطائي يقصد الغلبة ولا يقصد الإفادة أصلا. وكذلك المفاوضة الامتحانية والمحاورة العنادية. ولو أريد من الجدل الدلالة على الصدق لما كانت الصناعة متجهة إلى المتقابلات ومبنية على المسلمات. وحيث يراد به إقناع التعلم في المبادئ فليس يراد أن يفاد تصديقا جزئيا بل الغرض فيه أن يزيل عن نفس المتعلّم الاستذكار، ويشعره قرب الوضع من الإمكان، وتميل فطنته إلى طرف واحد من طرفي النقيض. وإن كان من العامي تصديقا فليس يكون من الخاصي تصديقا فإن تصديق الخاصي إنما هو بالحق ولا تصديق له بما فيه إمكان عناد. وأما تصديق العامي فليس من شرط أن يمحق الريب معه، ولذلك من بيانه أن يقول لمخاطبه صدقت.
The third type: on rhetorical syllogisms. As for rhetorical syllogisms, they are those that aim to persuade the public regarding what it is right for them to believe, and to benefit by a benefit that returns from them to wisdom or to dialectic. And since the addressee is a human, and a human is either elite or common. The elite does not benefit, insofar as he needs to believe the belief of the elite, except by demonstration, and the common does not benefit, insofar as he needs to believe the belief of the common, except by rhetoric. So the useful art by which a person acquires beneficial belief is either demonstration or rhetoric. As for dialectic, benefit is by achieving the goal, which is victory and compelling, as has passed; and the intention of victory is not the same as the intention of producing belief, for the sophist intends victory and does not intend to produce [belief] at all. Similarly, the examination discussion and the obstinate debate. And if dialectic were intended for indication of truth, then the art would not be directed to opposites and built on accepted premises. And when it is intended for persuasion of the learner in the principles, it is not intended to produce a particular belief; rather the purpose is to remove from the soul of the learner memorization, and make him feel the closeness of the position to possibility, and make his acumen incline to one side of the two opposites. And if there is belief from the common, it is not belief from the elite, for the belief of the elite is only by truth, and he has no belief in what involves the possibility of obstinacy. As for the belief of the common, it is not a condition that doubt be eliminated with it; therefore, from its explanation is that one says to his interlocutor 'you have spoken truthfully.'
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of syllogism: rhetorical syllogisms (al-qiyasat al-khitabiyya). The author explains that rhetoric is used to persuade the general public (al-'awamm) about what they should believe, and it provides a benefit that returns to wisdom or dialectic. The addressee is either elite (khassi) or common ('ammi). The elite can only be convinced by demonstration (burhan), while the common can only be convinced by rhetoric. Thus, the two useful arts for acquiring beneficial belief are demonstration and rhetoric. Dialectic (jadal) is different: its goal is victory and compelling the opponent, not necessarily producing belief. The sophist seeks victory without any intention of truth. Similarly, examination discussion and obstinate debate are not aimed at belief. If dialectic were intended for truth, it would not be directed to opposites and based on accepted premises. When dialectic is used to persuade a learner about principles, the purpose is not to produce a particular belief but to remove memorization, make the learner feel the closeness of the position to possibility, and incline his acumen to one side of the two opposites. Finally, the author distinguishes between belief of the elite and belief of the common: the elite's belief is only by truth and does not accept what involves possibility of obstinacy; the common's belief does not require elimination of doubt, hence in rhetoric one might simply say 'you have spoken truthfully' to the interlocutor.

Key Terms
خطابية
برهان
جدل
خاصي
عامي

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical syllogisms persuade the common public, demonstration proves to the elite, and dialectic seeks victory; the choice of argument depends on the audience.

Rhetoric: The Art of Persuasion and Its Three Types
Author defines rhetoric and classifies rhetorical speeches into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of rhetorical speech (pillar, trick, support) and identify them in real Islamic oratorical examples.

Core Idea
Rhetoric aims at persuasion, not truth, and uses three tools: pillar, trick, and support.

This passage from the logical tradition clarifies the scope of rhetoric. Unlike demonstration or dialectic, rhetoric's sole goal is persuasion. The author then categorizes rhetorical speeches into three types based on their intent: direct assent, emotional manipulation, or reinforcement of pre-existing belief. Understanding this classification helps you analyze speeches, sermons, and debates in Islamic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall purpose of rhetoric
In the first sentence, the author states that rhetoric is not about truth or justice but about persuasion. Mark this as the unique goal that distinguishes rhetoric from other logical arts.
2
Break down the means of persuasion
Persuasive things are either statements (qawl) or testimonies (shahada). Testimonies are further divided. Note the role of non-syllogistic persuasion.
3
Focus on the threefold classification of rhetorical speeches
The author summarizes three kinds: pillar (direct assent), trick (emotional/deception), support (reinforcement). Write down their definitions.
Pillar: aims at direct assent to the claim. Trick: aims to evoke emotion or deceive. Support: reinforces an already accepted claim.
4
Apply the classification
Consider a real Islamic sermon and categorize each part as pillar, trick, or support. For example, a Qur'anic verse used as evidence is pillar; an emotional appeal is trick; citing a companion's agreement is support.
5
Evaluate the author's stance on the schools
The author favors the school that allows all three types. Reflect on why restricting to only pillar might be too narrow for effective rhetoric.

لأنه لما لم يكن الغرض في الخطابة إصابة الحق ولا إلزام العدل بل الإقناع وحده كان كل مقنع مناسبًا للغرض. ولا يلزم أن يكون المقنع قولاً قياسياً أو ما يجري مجراه ؛ فإنك قد تقنع بما يحكم به المعروف بالصدق. والأشياء المقنعة إما قول وإما شهادة؛ والشهادة إما شهادة قول مثل الاستشهاد بقول نبي أو إمام أو حكيم مثلا، وإما شهادة حال. والحال إما حال يُدرك بالعقل منه فضيلة القائل واشتهاره بالصدق والتمييز، وإما حال يُدرك بالحس؛ وذلك إما قول مثل التحدي واليمين والعهد، وإما غير قول مثل من تحير ببشارة وسخت و جهه بسخته فمنه ما تكون الحال الشاهدة تتبع الانفعال النفساني مثل السخت والهيبة فمنه ما يكون الحال الشاهدة طارئة من خارج مثل العقوبة أو المبرة. واختلاف الأقوال بجهة رفع الصوت وخفضه ظاهر. وكذلك اختلاف الأقوال بجهة ما يكون بالتكلُّف وغير التكلُّف حسيّا كان أو عقليًا. وقد تكون في الخطابة أقوال غير العمود الذي مرَّ ذكره مثل ما يراد به إيجاب التصديق بمقتضى الأمور المذكورة أو يراد به تقرير الفضيلة كمن تقرر فضيلته عند السامعين ليصدقوا بها . وبالجملة فالأقاويل الخطابية التي يراد بها التصديق ثلاثة أصناف؛ العمود، والحيلة، والنُّصرة. [١] أما العمود فهو الذي يراد به التصديق بالمطلوب نفسه. [٢] وأما الحيلة فهي التي يراد بها انفعال شيء أو إيهام الخلق. [٣] وأما النصرة فهو قول ينصر به ما له تصديق؛ فقد اتضح لك إذن أن هنا شيئًا هو العمود، وشيئًا هو خارج عنه، وأن جميع هذه صناعي. وذُكر في التعليم الأول «إن السلف في أصول الخطابة لم يتعرضوا لأحكام تكلموا فيها متعلقة بالأمور الخارجية بل تعرضوا لأفعال انفعالية وخلقية وغير ذلك مما هو غير العمود؛ إذ ليس الغرض فيه نفس التصديق بل الغرض فيه استدراج السامع. وكان مذهب الخطباء في ذلك الزمان مذهبان: [أ] مذهب يختص به بلاد من بلادهم يسوغ الخطيب استعمال كل مقنع من العمود ومن الحيلة ومن النصرة [ب] ومذهب لا يسوغ أن يشتغل لشيء سوى القول المقنع؛ والصواب هو المذهب الأول، والله أعلم.
Because since the purpose in rhetoric is not attaining truth nor imposing justice but persuasion alone, every persuasive thing is suitable for the purpose. And it is not necessary that the persuasive thing be a syllogistic statement or what runs its course; for you may persuade by what is judged by one known for truthfulness. Persuasive things are either statements or testimonies; testimony is either testimony of a statement, such as citing the statement of a prophet, imam, or sage for example, or testimony of a state. The state is either a state perceived by the intellect from which the virtue of the speaker and his renown for truthfulness and discernment are derived, or a state perceived by sense; that is either a statement such as a challenge, an oath, and a covenant, or non-statement such as one who is disturbed by good news and anger and direction with his anger. From it, the witnessing state may follow psychological emotion such as anger and awe; from it, the witnessing state may be incidental from outside such as punishment or kindness. The variation of statements in respect of raising and lowering the voice is obvious. Likewise, the variation of statements in respect of being with effort and without effort is apparent, whether sensory or intellectual. And there may be in rhetoric statements other than the pillar that has been mentioned, such as what is intended to produce assent according to the aforementioned matters or what is intended to establish virtue, as one whose virtue is established in the ears of the listeners so that they believe him. In summary, rhetorical speeches intended for assent are three kinds: the pillar, the trick, and the support. [1] As for the pillar, it is what is intended for assent to the very thing sought. [2] As for the trick, it is what is intended for the emotion of something or deception of people. [3] As for the support, it is a statement by which what already has assent is supported. It has become clear to you then that here there is something that is the pillar, something outside it, and that all these are two crafts? And it was mentioned in the first teaching that the predecessors in the principles of rhetoric did not attend to rulings that they spoke about pertaining to external matters, but rather attended to emotional, moral, and other things that are other than the pillar, since the purpose is not assent itself but the purpose is to entice the listener. The school of the orators at that time was two schools: [A] a school specific to some of their lands that permits the orator to use every persuasive thing from the pillar, the trick, and the support; [B] a school that does not permit occupying oneself with anything other than the persuasive statement. The correct is the first school, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the means of persuasion in rhetoric (khataba). The author begins by asserting that the goal of rhetoric is not truth or justice but pure persuasion. Therefore, any convincing means is acceptable, including those that are not syllogistic. He then divides persuasive things into statements (qawl) and testimonies (shahada). Testimony is further divided into testimony of a statement (e.g., quoting a prophet) and testimony of a state (hal). The state can be intellectual (perceived by reason, showing the speaker's virtue) or sensory (perceived by senses). Sensory testimony can be verbal (challenge, oath, covenant) or non-verbal (e.g., signs of emotion like anger or awe). The author notes variations in delivery like raising/lowering voice. He then summarizes that rhetorical speeches for assent are three: the pillar (al-‘amud) which aims at direct assent to the proposition, the trick (al-hila) which aims to evoke emotion or deception, and the support (al-nusra) which supports an already accepted claim. The passage ends by mentioning two historical schools: one permitting all three means, the other restricting to persuasive statements only, with the author favoring the first. For example, a sermon urging charity using a verse from the Qur'an as support would be an instance of al-nusra, while a direct logical proof would be al-‘amud.

Key Terms

إقناع
عمود
حيلة
نصرة

Checkpoint
In a sermon urging charity, a speaker says: 'Zakat is obligatory because the Qur'an commands it, and the Prophet practiced it. Therefore, you must give zakat.' Which type of rhetorical speech does this illustrate?
A) The pillar (al-‘amud)
B) The trick (al-hila)
C) The support (al-nusra)
D) Testimony (shahada)

Key Takeaway
The three rhetorical types—pillar, trick, support—cover the full range of persuasive speeches in rhetoric.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classifying Judgments for the Rhetorician
Author lists the types of things a rhetorician must establish and categorizes judgments by their source and scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify rulings (ahkam) according to their source (reason vs. revelation) and their scope (universal vs. particular) as a rhetorician must.

Core Idea
A rhetorician must know whether a judgment comes from reason, revelation, or both, and whether it applies to all, some nations, or a single person.

The rhetorician speaks about particular matters and must prove various claims. But not all claims are alike: some are based on reason, some on revelation, and some on both. Moreover, some claims are universally accepted, while others are specific to certain communities or individuals. In this lesson, we examine the author's classification of judgments, which equips the rhetorician to choose the appropriate persuasive strategy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rhetorician's domain
The passage opens by stating that the rhetorician speaks about particular matters (al-umur al-juz'iyya). Note that rhetoric deals with specifics, not universals. The rhetorician must first establish existence or non-existence in three time frames.
Particular matters are the domain of rhetoric, as opposed to the universal matters of demonstration.
2
Distinguish existential from evaluative claims
The author separates claims about existence (wujud/'adam) from claims about value (good/evil, beneficial/harmful, virtue/vice). For evaluative claims, the rhetorician may or may not need to prove them, depending on the context.
This distinction influences whether the rhetorician uses logical proof or persuasive appeal.
3
Analyze the three-way division by source
The passage divides rulings (ahkam) into: (a) shared between reason and scripture (attributed to reason in custom), (b) specific to scripture (attributed to the Lawgiver alone), and (c) specific to reason (attributed to reason alone). Find the examples given for each.
Example of shared: 'kindness to parents is obligatory'. Example of scripture-specific: prayer, fasting. Example of reason-specific: considering consequences, reflection.
4
Analyze the three-way division by scope
Further, rulings are divided by scope: general (agreed by all rational people, e.g., justice is virtuous), particular to certain peoples (e.g., differing schools of thought), and particular to certain individuals (e.g., the ruling of an imam).
Scope determines whether the rhetorician can appeal to universal consensus or must address specific audiences.
5
Apply the classification to a sample ruling
Take a statement like 'Zakat is obligatory'. Classify it by source: scripture-specific. By scope: general for Muslims, but not for non-Muslims. For a Muslim audience, the rhetorician would cite revelation; for a non-Muslim, reason would not establish it.
Practical use: the rhetorician tailors the argument to the audience and the type of judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل خطيب يتكلم في الأمور الجزئية [١] فإنه يحتاج إلى أن يثبت كون الشيء موجودًا أو غير موجود في الحاضر أو الماضي أو المستقبل. [٢] وأما كون ذلك الشيء خيرًا أو شرا أو نافعا أو ضارًا أو فضيلة أو رذيلة فربما لزمه أن يثبت، وربَّما لم يلزمه؛ [أ] فإن من الأحكام ما يكون مشتركا بين العقل والشرع فينسب إلى العقل في العرف وإن كان مشروعًا مثل قولهم الإحسان إلى الآباء واجب وشكر المنعم فرض ونحو ذلك. [ب] ومنها ما لا يكون مشتركا بل يكون مخصوصا بالشرع فينسب إلى الشارع لا غير كالصوم والصلاة والزكاة والحج ؛ فإنه وإن كان على وفق العقل في الحقيقة فلا ينسب إلى العقل . [ت] ومنها ما يكون مخصوصا بالعقل فينسب إلى العقل لا غير نحو النظر في عواقب الأمور والتفكر في حقائقها والمبالغة في الرياضة طلبا للوصول إلى المقاصد ؛ والرياضة قد تكون بالذكر في الخلوة، وقد تكون بالفكر، وقد تكون بتقليل الطعام، وقد تكون بتقليل الكلام، وقد تكون بالبعض منها مع البعض، وقد تكون بالكل جميعًا. [ث] ثمَّ من الأحكام ما يكون عاما يمكن أن يثبته كل عاقل وذلك في الأمور المتفق عليها كفضيلة العدل ورذيلة الجور مثلا. [ج] ومنها ما لا يكون عاماً بل يكون خاصا بالبعض من الأقوام دون البعض كما في المذاهب المختلفة، [ح] ومنها ما يكون مخصوصا بالبعض من الأشخاص دون البعض كحكم الإمام ونائبه.
Every rhetorician speaks about particular matters. He needs to establish whether something exists or does not exist in the present, past, or future. As for whether that thing is good or evil, beneficial or harmful, virtue or vice, perhaps he is required to prove it, and perhaps not. For among rulings are those shared between reason and scripture, so they are attributed to reason in custom even if they are legislated, such as their saying: kindness to parents is obligatory and thanking the benefactor is a duty, etc. And among them are those not shared but specific to scripture, so they are attributed to the Lawgiver alone, such as fasting, prayer, zakat, and Hajj; for even if they are in accordance with reason in reality, they are not attributed to reason. And among them are those specific to reason, so they are attributed to reason alone, such as considering the consequences of matters, reflecting on their realities, and striving in spiritual exercise seeking to reach the goals; and the exercise may be through remembrance in solitude, through thought, through reducing food, through reducing speech, through some of these together, or through all of them. Then among rulings are those that are general, which every rational person can establish, such as the virtue of justice and the vice of injustice, for example. And among them are those not general but specific to some peoples but not others, as in different schools, and among them are those specific to some individuals rather than others, such as the ruling of the imam and his deputy.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the types of propositions and judgments that a rhetorician (khatib) must address when speaking about particular matters. The author first states that the rhetorician must establish existence (wujud) or non-existence ('adam) in three time frames. Then he turns to axiological judgments: good/evil, beneficial/harmful, virtue/vice, noting that sometimes proof is required, sometimes not. He then categorizes rulings (ahkam) by their source: (a) shared between reason and scripture, attributed to reason in custom (e.g., kindness to parents is obligatory); (b) specific to scripture, attributed solely to the Lawgiver (e.g., prayer, zakat); (c) specific to reason, attributed to reason alone (e.g., considering consequences, spiritual exercises). He further divides rulings by scope: general (accepted by all rational people, e.g., justice is virtuous), particular to certain peoples (e.g., differing schools), and particular to specific individuals (e.g., the imam's ruling). This classification helps the rhetorician choose the appropriate basis for persuasion.

Key Takeaway
A rhetorician must classify judgments by source (reason/revelation/both) and scope (universal/particular) to choose the correct mode of persuasion.

Dialectic vs Rhetoric: Universal and Particular in Argumentation
Contrast between dialectic and rhetoric

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between dialectic (jadal) and rhetoric (khataba) based on their scope and typical subject matter as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Dialectic deals with universals; rhetoric with particulars — a key distinction in the five arts.

In this passage, the author clarifies that rhetoric, like dialectic, is not group-specific, but the two arts differ in their primary domain: dialectic focuses on universal propositions, while rhetoric deals with particular cases. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying each art in Islamic disciplines such as fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two arts being compared
Locate where the passage mentions 'jadal' (dialectic) and 'khataba' (rhetoric). Note the author's statement that both are not specific to any one group.
2
Extract the defining characteristic of each
The passage says: 'Most judgments in dialectic concern universal matters, unlike rhetoric, for most of its judgments are in particular matters.' Identify the key terms: 'kulliyya' (universal) and 'juz'iyya' (particular).
3
Compare their reliance on universal laws
Even though rhetoric deals with particulars, it is not isolated from universal laws – as the passage later explains. Notice that rhetoric depends on the same universal principles as dialectic, but applies them to specific situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن يكون ذلك الحكم ضارًا أو نافعا أو محمودًا أو مذموما في الحقيقة فذلك أمر آخر؛ إذ الحكم على حسب الاعتقاد؛ والناس / في الأمور الاعتقادية مختلفة فحكم كل طائفة على حسب اعتقاد تلك الطائفة. وكما أن الجدل لا يكون مخصوصًا بطائفة دون طائفة فكذلك الخطابة؛ والأحكام قد تكون في الأمور الكلية مثل أن كل ما كان كذا فهو كذا، وقد تكون في الأمور الجزئية مثل أن يحكم على شيء معين بشيء معين في وقت معين. وأكثر أحكام الجدل في الأمور الكلية بخلاف الخطابة فإن أكثر أحكامها في الأمور الجزئية. والأحكام إذا كانت شرعية فالقوانين الكلية موكولة إلى وضع الشارع والقوانين الكلية من اللوازم؛ إذ لا سبيل إلى الحكم في جزئي جزئي بعينه مما لا يتناهى. والحكم الكلي هو شيء مفروغ عنه ليس مما يستأنف إثباته وإن كان مستأنفا إثباته فليس للحيل الاستدراجية في تصحيحه مدخل. ثم النظر في الخطابة وإن كان في الأمور الجزئية فلا يكون بمعزل عن القوانين الكلية سواء كانت تلك الأمور من الأمور الشرعية أو العقلية، والأحكام اللازمة في جميع الأمور هي الأحكام السياسية؛ والسياسية إما شرعية وإما عقلية والعقلية إما خاصة وإما عامة يحصل منها تدبير الملك بحكم ما يكون الحكم في يده كما في الشريعة بحكم الشارع، أو بحكم بانيه وهو القاضي وإلا لا تكون الأحوال على سبيل الاستقامة؛ ولا ينقطع الخصام بين الخصوم. والاستقامة في الأمور قد تكون بحسب الأمور الظاهرة، وقد تكون بحسب الأمور الباطنة، وذلك بالخوف عما يزجره من العقوبات العاجلة والآجلة، أو بالرجاء في الوصول إلى المطلوبات كذلك.
As for that judgment being actually harmful or beneficial, praiseworthy or blameworthy, that is another matter; since judgment is according to belief; and people differ in matters of belief, so each group's judgment is according to its belief. And just as dialectic is not specific to one group over another, likewise rhetoric. Judgments may be in universal matters, like 'everything that is such is such', and may be in particular matters, like judging a specific thing with a specific thing at a specific time. Most judgments in dialectic concern universal matters, unlike rhetoric, for most of its judgments are in particular matters. If judgments are legal, then universal laws are entrusted to the Lawgiver's institution, and universal laws are among necessary things; for there is no way to judge every particular particular ad infinitum. The universal judgment is something already settled, not something whose proof is sought anew, and even if its proof is sought anew, there is no room for persuasive devices in its correction. Then consideration in rhetoric, although it concerns particular matters, is not isolated from universal laws, whether those matters are legal or rational. The necessary judgments in all matters are political judgments; political judgments are either legal or rational, and rational ones are either specific or universal, from which governance of the kingdom is attained according to the rule that the judgment is in his hand, as in the law by the lawgiver's rule, or by the rule of its founder, which is the judge, otherwise affairs would not be upright; and disputes between litigants would not cease. Uprightness in matters may be according to apparent matters, and may be according to inner matters, that is through fear of deterrent punishments immediate and deferred, or through hope of attaining desired things likewise.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage further analyzes judgments in rhetoric. First, the actual harm or benefit of a judgment depends on belief, which varies among groups. Rhetoric, like dialectic, is not group-specific. Then it contrasts dialectic (mostly universal judgments) with rhetoric (mostly particular). It explains that universal legal judgments are fixed by the Lawgiver, and particular judgments cannot be enumerated infinitely, so universal principles are necessary. Rhetoric, though dealing with particulars, relies on universal laws. Finally, it introduces political judgments as necessary for governance, dividing them into legal and rational, and rational into specific and universal. These ensure uprightness through fear of punishment or hope of reward, both externally and internally.

جدل
خطابة
أمور كلية
أمور جزئية

C) Dialectic uses trickery, rhetoric does not
D) Rhetoric is only for legal matters, dialectic for rational

Key Takeaway
Dialectic and rhetoric are distinguished primarily by their scope: dialectic focuses on universal propositions, rhetoric on particular judgments.

Political Judgments: Universal Laws and Governance
Definition and classification of political judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify political judgments (siyasiyya) into legal and rational types, and explain their dependence on universal laws as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Political judgments are necessary for governance, rooted in universal laws, and ensure uprightness through fear and hope.

After discussing rhetoric, the author turns to political judgments, which he describes as necessary in all matters. He explains that these judgments, whether legal or rational, must be grounded in universal laws (qawanin kulliyya) to avoid endless particularities. The passage also highlights the role of the lawgiver and judge in maintaining order through deterrence and incentive.

Guided Path
1
Locate the author's claim about political judgments
Find the sentence: 'The necessary judgments in all matters are political judgments.' This is the central thesis of this section.
2
Break down the classification of political judgments
The author divides political judgments into legal (shar'iyya) and rational ('aqliyya). Rational ones further split into specific (khassa) and universal ('amma). Identify these categories in the Arabic text.
3
Understand the role of universal laws
The passage states that universal laws are essential because particular judgments cannot be enumerated infinitely. Universal principles are 'already settled' and provide the foundation for judging particular cases.
4
Explain how uprightness is achieved
The author mentions uprightness (istiqama) through fear of punishment (khawf) and hope of reward (raja'), both in apparent and inner matters. This is linked to the authority of the lawgiver or judge.

وأما أن يكون ذلك الحكم ضارًا أو نافعا أو محمودًا أو مذموما في الحقيقة فذلك أمر آخر؛ إذ الحكم على حسب الاعتقاد؛ والناس / في الأمور الاعتقادية مختلفة فحكم كل طائفة على حسب اعتقاد تلك الطائفة. وكما أن الجدل لا يكون مخصوصًا بطائفة دون طائفة فكذلك الخطابة؛ والأحكام قد تكون في الأمور الكلية مثل أن كل ما كان كذا فهو كذا، وقد تكون في الأمور الجزئية مثل أن يحكم على شيء معين بشيء معين في وقت معين. وأكثر أحكام الجدل في الأمور الكلية بخلاف الخطابة فإن أكثر أحكامها في الأمور الجزئية. والأحكام إذا كانت شرعية فالقوانين الكلية موكولة إلى وضع الشارع والقوانين الكلية من اللوازم؛ إذ لا سبيل إلى الحكم في جزئي جزئي بعينه مما لا يتناهى. والحكم الكلي هو شيء مفروغ عنه ليس مما يستأنف إثباته وإن كان مستأنفا إثباته فليس للحيل الاستدراجية في تصحيحه مدخل. ثم النظر في الخطابة وإن كان في الأمور الجزئية فلا يكون بمعزل عن القوانين الكلية سواء كانت تلك الأمور من الأمور الشرعية أو العقلية، والأحكام اللازمة في جميع الأمور هي الأحكام السياسية؛ والسياسية إما شرعية وإما عقلية والعقلية إما خاصة وإما عامة يحصل منها تدبير الملك بحكم ما يكون الحكم في يده كما في الشريعة بحكم الشارع، أو بحكم بانيه وهو القاضي وإلا لا تكون الأحوال على سبيل الاستقامة؛ ولا ينقطع الخصام بين الخصوم. والاستقامة في الأمور قد تكون بحسب الأمور الظاهرة، وقد تكون بحسب الأمور الباطنة، وذلك بالخوف عما يزجره من العقوبات العاجلة والآجلة، أو بالرجاء في الوصول إلى المطلوبات كذلك.
As for that judgment being actually harmful or beneficial, praiseworthy or blameworthy, that is another matter; since judgment is according to belief; and people differ in matters of belief, so each group's judgment is according to its belief. And just as dialectic is not specific to one group over another, likewise rhetoric. Judgments may be in universal matters, like 'everything that is such is such', and may be in particular matters, like judging a specific thing with a specific thing at a specific time. Most judgments in dialectic concern universal matters, unlike rhetoric, for most of its judgments are in particular matters. If judgments are legal, then universal laws are entrusted to the Lawgiver's institution, and universal laws are among necessary things; for there is no way to judge every particular particular ad infinitum. The universal judgment is something already settled, not something whose proof is sought anew, and even if its proof is sought anew, there is no room for persuasive devices in its correction. Then consideration in rhetoric, although it concerns particular matters, is not isolated from universal laws, whether those matters are legal or rational. The necessary judgments in all matters are political judgments; political judgments are either legal or rational, and rational ones are either specific or universal, from which governance of the kingdom is attained according to the rule that the judgment is in his hand, as in the law by the lawgiver's rule, or by the rule of its founder, which is the judge, otherwise affairs would not be upright; and disputes between litigants would not cease. Uprightness in matters may be according to apparent matters, and may be according to inner matters, that is through fear of deterrent punishments immediate and deferred, or through hope of attaining desired things likewise.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage further analyzes judgments in rhetoric. First, the actual harm or benefit of a judgment depends on belief, which varies among groups. Rhetoric, like dialectic, is not group-specific. Then it contrasts dialectic (mostly universal judgments) with rhetoric (mostly particular). It explains that universal legal judgments are fixed by the Lawgiver, and particular judgments cannot be enumerated infinitely, so universal principles are necessary. Rhetoric, though dealing with particulars, relies on universal laws. Finally, it introduces political judgments as necessary for governance, dividing them into legal and rational, and rational into specific and universal. These ensure uprightness through fear of punishment or hope of reward, both externally and internally.

شرعية

قوانين كلية

According to the passage, what are the two main categories of political judgments?
A) Universal and particular
B) Legal and rational
C) Apparent and inner
D) Dialectical and rhetorical

Key Takeaway
Political judgments, whether legal or rational, are essential for governance and rely on universal principles to ensure uprightness in society.
