Rhetoric vs Dialectic: The Premise Criterion
Author distinguishes rhetoric from dialectic by premise type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rhetorical and dialectical syllogisms based on the nature of their praiseworthy premises.

Core Idea
Rhetoric uses outwardly praiseworthy premises; dialectic uses genuinely praiseworthy ones.

In this passage, the author clarifies a foundational distinction between rhetoric (khataba) and dialectic (jadal). Both use praiseworthy premises (mamdudat), but the standard differs: dialectic relies on genuine praiseworthiness, while rhetoric relies on what appears praiseworthy at first glance. This distinction determines their respective domains and limitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two arts
Locate where the author defines dialectic and rhetoric based on the type of premises they use.
Look for the terms 'جدلي' and 'خطابي' and their associated descriptions.
2
Extract the premise criterion
What distinguishes a 'genuinely praiseworthy' premise from one that is praiseworthy 'according to outward opinion'?
The author explicitly states: 'الجدلي هو الذي يكون من محمودات حقيقية، والخطابي هو الذي يكون من محمودات بحسب بادي الرأي'.
3
Compare with demonstration
How does demonstration (burhan) differ from both? It does not rely on praiseworthiness but on truth.
4
Apply to a real scenario
Given an argument from Islamic jurisprudence, determine whether it would be classified as rhetorical or dialectical based on its premises.
For example, an argument using a widely accepted custom (urf) might be rhetorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكل واحد منهما طرق كثيرة وأسباب متعددة. والخطابة لم تكون بها قوة أن يتكلف الإقناع في جميعها بل الخطابة إنما هي بالضمير وما يحسن به الضمير ؛ ويفخم به شأن الدليل هو تعقل العمدة التي عليها العمل؛ إذ العمل لا يعتبر لا عقلا ولا شرعًا إلا وأن يكون على قانون لا يكون ذلك على خلاف العقل والدليل. وأما الأحكام التشاورية فقيل ما يفترض فيها قوانين من عند أصحاب الشرائع إلا في أمور عنادية وجهادية على خلاف الأحكام التشاجرية؛ وليس لكل واحد في كل حكم بصيرة بل البعض من الأحكام مخصوص بالعض كما مرّ.
وبالجملة كأنه يجب صرف الشغل إلى الآلة الصناعية الأهلية وهو ما يوقع التصديق من حيث هو موقع للتصديق بالقياس المحذوف كبراه؛ وهو الذي يسمَّى تفكيرًا أو ضميرًا. وقد عرفت أن التفكير جزء من قياس ذلك القياس إذا تم كان مظنونا به أنه جدلي؛ وذلك لأن الجدلي هو الذي يكون من محمودات حقيقية، والخطابي هو الذي يكون من محمودات بحسب بادي الرأي. ولما كان النظر في القياس الجدلي الذي يسمى منطقيا لصناعة المنطق . فالنظر في الشبيه به لصناعة إذا كان النظر في الحق، وما يشبه الحق لصناعة واحدة؛ فالنظر في المحمودات / حقًا، والمحمودات ظنا، وفي استعمالها لصناعة واحدة كما أن النظر في الصادق. والحق الذي منه ينبعث البرهان، وفي المحمود الذي منه ينبعث الجدل لصناعة واحدة إذا كانت الصناعة المنطقية بالاستحقاق الأول هو البرهان؛ وكان الجدل شبيها به إذا كانت المحمودات تشبه الحق . وغرائز الناس مشغوفة بالتماس الحق لكن السبيل إليه صعب؛ فمنهم من يوفّق له، ومنهم من يضع إلى التشبيه به.
فصل في مشاركات الخطابة لغيرها من الصنائع ومخالفاتها له
إن صناعة الخطابة عظيمة النفع جدا؛ وذلك لأن الأحكام الصادقة فيما عدل وحسن أفضل نفعًا وأعم على الناس جدوى من أضدادها؛ وذلك لأن نوع الإنسان مشتق بالتشارك، والتشارك محوج إلى التعامل والتجاور؛ والتعامل والتجاور يحوّجان إلى أحكام صادقة في الأمور العلمية التي بها ينتظم شمل المصلحة؛ وهذه الأحكام تحتاج أن تكون مقررة في النفوس متمكنة من العقائد وقد بينا أن البرهان قليل الجدوى في حمل الجمهور على العقد الحق، وبينا أن الخطابة هي المتكلفة فيه؛ فإحدى فضائل هذه الصناعة عيادها في تقرير هذه الأعراض في الأنفس. وأيضًا فإن في الأمور الجزئية أحكامًا يوجبها التعقل الصحيح؛ وليس التعقل الصحيح مبنيا على المخاطبة والمحاورة بل قانونه الروية والنظر كما أن البرهان في الأمور الكلية النظرية مبني على الحق دون المحاورة . ثمَّ الذي يصح بالبرهان من تلك الأمور إذا أريد تقريره في نفس من تسفّل عن رتبة البرهان كان الجدل أعون شيء على تقريره كذلك المدرك بالتعقل إذا أريد أن يقرر في نفس من يضعف عن العقل بنفسه كانت الخطابة أعون شيء عليه.
وإذا لم يكن المدبر من الناس مستحقاً لأن يخاطب بالصحيح من البيان العلمي فيما ينبغي أن يعتقد، أو بالبيان التعقلي فيما ينبغي أن يعمل : فإذا كانت لنا قوة خطابية تمكننا من إقناع المخاطب بما يحمده ويظنه ويقبلها ويناسب قدره ويشاكله فصناعة الخطابة من الصنائع التي يقنع بها في المتضادين كما أن صناعة الجدل كانت صناعة يقاس بها على المتضادين. وليس على أن الخطابة يقنع بها في وقت واحد أن هذا الشيء بعينه كان؛ وإنه بعينه لم يكن، ولا على
And each of the two has many methods and numerous causes. Rhetoric does not have the power to undertake persuasion in all of them, but rhetoric is only by inner conscience and what embellishes the inner conscience; and what magnifies the status of the proof is the understanding of the pillar upon which action depends; for action is considered neither rationally nor legally unless it is according to a law that is not contrary to reason and proof. As for consultative judgments, it is said that laws are prescribed from the people of the Sharia except in matters of obstinacy and jihad, contrary to contentious judgments; and not everyone has insight into every judgment, but some judgments are specific to certain individuals as previously mentioned. In summary, it seems that one must direct effort to the appropriate industrial tool, which is what produces assent (tasdiq) insofar as it is a producer of assent through the syllogism with its major premise omitted; and this is called thinking (tafkir) or inner conscience (damir). You have known that thinking is part of the syllogism of that syllogism; if that syllogism is completed, it is considered probable that it is dialectical; because the dialectical is that which consists of genuinely praiseworthy premises, while the rhetorical is that which consists of praiseworthy premises according to outward opinion. Since the consideration of the dialectical syllogism, which is called logical for the art of logic, then the consideration of what resembles it for the art — if the consideration is of truth and what resembles truth — for one art; so the consideration of praiseworthy premises in truth and praiseworthy premises in opinion, and their use, is for one art just as the consideration of the true. And truth from which demonstration emanates, and the praiseworthy from which dialectic emanates, are for one art if the logical art in primary merit is demonstration; and dialectic is similar to it when the praiseworthy premises resemble truth. Human instincts are passionate about seeking truth, but the path to it is difficult; some are guided to it, and some fall into what resembles it.

Chapter on the participations of rhetoric with other arts and its differences from them.
Indeed the art of rhetoric is of great benefit; that is because truthful judgments in matters of justice and goodness are more beneficial and more widespread among people than their opposites; this is because the human species is intertwined through partnership, and partnership necessitates interaction and coexistence; interaction and coexistence necessitate truthful judgments in scientific matters that organize the common interest; these judgments need to be settled in souls and firmly established in beliefs. We have shown that demonstration is of little use in leading the masses to true belief, and we have shown that rhetoric is the one that undertakes this; thus one of the virtues of this art is its role in establishing these attributes in souls. Also, in particular matters, there are judgments required by correct reasoning; correct reasoning is not based on address and dialogue but its law is reflection and consideration, just as demonstration in universal theoretical matters is based on truth without dialogue. Then, what is established by demonstration from those matters, if one wishes to establish it in a soul that has descended from the rank of demonstration, dialectic is the most helpful thing in establishing it; similarly, what is grasped by correct reasoning, if one wishes to establish it in a soul that is weak in reasoning by itself, rhetoric is the most helpful thing in it. And if the manager of the people is not entitled to address them with correct scientific discourse regarding what should be believed, or with rational discourse regarding what should be done: then if we have a rhetorical ability that enables us to persuade the interlocutor with what he praises and opines, accepts, and matches his capacity and resembles him, then the art of rhetoric is one of the arts by which one is persuaded concerning opposites, just as the art of dialectic was an art by which one measures concerning opposites. But not that rhetoric persuades at one time that this very thing was and that it was not, nor that...
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage is a comparative epistemological discussion of rhetoric (khataba) relative to dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan). The author begins by noting that each art has multiple methods, but rhetoric does not have the power to persuade in all matters; rather, it works through the inner conscience (damir) and what embellishes it. The proof's importance is tied to the foundation of action, which must conform to reason and divine law. He then distinguishes consultative judgments (ahkam tashawuriyya) from contentious ones, noting that some judgments are specific to certain individuals. 

The author then turns to the core distinction: the art of logic primarily deals with demonstration, but there are approximations. Dialectic uses genuinely praiseworthy premises (mamdudat haqiqiyya), while rhetoric uses premises that are praiseworthy according to outward opinion (mamdudat bihasb badi' al-ra'y). Thinking (tafkir) is a part of a syllogism whose major premise is omitted, and this syllogism is considered probable and resembles dialectic if based on genuine praiseworthiness. The consideration of truth and its resemblance belongs to one art, just as the consideration of the true and the praiseworthy in opinion is for one art. 

He highlights that human nature is eager for truth but the path is difficult; some find it, others fall into its resemblance. Then in the new chapter section, he explains the great benefit of rhetoric: truthful judgments are more beneficial and widespread because humans are social, needing interaction and coexistence which require truthful judgments in scientific matters. These judgments must be firmly established in souls. Demonstration is ineffective for the masses, so rhetoric undertakes this task. Similarly, for particular matters requiring correct reasoning (ta'aqqul), rhetoric is most helpful for those weak in reasoning. Finally, rhetoric can be used to persuade concerning opposites, like dialectic, but not to assert contradictory propositions simultaneously. This passage clarifies the epistemological role of rhetoric as a necessary tool for disseminating truth among those incapable of demonstration.

الجدل

What is the fundamental difference between rhetorical and dialectical premises according to the passage?
A) Rhetoric uses true premises; dialectic uses false premises.
B) Rhetoric uses genuinely praiseworthy premises; dialectic uses widely accepted premises.
C) Dialectic uses genuinely praiseworthy premises; rhetoric uses premises praiseworthy by outward opinion.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental difference between rhetoric and dialectic is the nature of their praiseworthy premises: genuine vs. outward opinion.

The Social Necessity of Rhetoric
Author argues for rhetoric's social utility compared to demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why rhetoric is necessary for establishing true beliefs in the general public.

Core Idea
Rhetoric is essential because demonstration cannot reach the masses.

After distinguishing rhetoric from dialectic, the author argues for rhetoric's great benefit. He explains that human society depends on truthful judgments, but demonstration is ineffective for most people. Therefore rhetoric, with its persuasive power based on what is commonly accepted, is the primary tool for instilling correct beliefs and actions in the community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
What is the limitation of demonstration (burhan) according to the author?
Find the phrase 'البرهان قليل الجدوى في حمل الجمهور على العقد الحق'.
2
Find the social need
Why do people need truthful judgments? The author mentions human interdependence and interaction.
Look for 'التشارك' and 'التعامل والتجاور'.
3
See rhetoric's role
How does rhetoric fill the gap? It is the art that undertakes the task of establishing these judgments in souls.
4
Compare with dialectic
When is dialectic more helpful than rhetoric? For those who have descended from the rank of demonstration but can still grasp genuine praiseworthiness.

فصل في مشاركات الخطابة لغيرها من الصنائع ومخالفاتها له
إن صناعة الخطابة عظيمة النفع جدا؛ وذلك لأن الأحكام الصادقة فيما عدل وحسن أفضل نفعًا وأعم على الناس جدوى من أضدادها؛ وذلك لأن نوع الإنسان مشتق بالتشارك، والتشارك محوج إلى التعامل والتجاور؛ والتعامل والتجاور يحوّجان إلى أحكام صادقة في الأمور العلمية التي بها ينتظم شمل المصلحة؛ وهذه الأحكام تحتاج أن تكون مقررة في النفوس متمكنة من العقائد وقد بينا أن البرهان قليل الجدوى في حمل الجمهور على العقد الحق، وبينا أن الخطابة هي المتكلفة فيه؛ فإحدى فضائل هذه الصناعة عيادها في تقرير هذه الأعراض في الأنفس. وأيضًا فإن في الأمور الجزئية أحكامًا يوجبها التعقل الصحيح؛ وليس التعقل الصحيح مبنيا على المخاطبة والمحاورة بل قانونه الروية والنظر كما أن البرهان في الأمور الكلية النظرية مبني على الحق دون المحاورة . ثمَّ الذي يصح بالبرهان من تلك الأمور إذا أريد تقريره في نفس من تسفّل عن رتبة البرهان كان الجدل أعون شيء على تقريره كذلك المدرك بالتعقل إذا أريد أن يقرر في نفس من يضعف عن العقل بنفسه كانت الخطابة أعون شيء عليه.
وإذا لم يكن المدبر من الناس مستحقاً لأن يخاطب بالصحيح من البيان العلمي فيما ينبغي أن يعتقد، أو بالبيان التعقلي فيما ينبغي أن يعمل : فإذا كانت لنا قوة خطابية تمكننا من إقناع المخاطب بما يحمده ويظنه ويقبلها ويناسب قدره ويشاكله فصناعة الخطابة من الصنائع التي يقنع بها في المتضادين كما أن صناعة الجدل كانت صناعة يقاس بها على المتضادين. وليس على أن الخطابة يقنع بها في وقت واحد أن هذا الشيء بعينه كان؛ وإنه بعينه لم يكن، ولا على
And each of the two has many methods and numerous causes. Rhetoric does not have the power to undertake persuasion in all of them, but rhetoric is only by inner conscience and what embellishes the inner conscience; and what magnifies the status of the proof is the understanding of the pillar upon which action depends; for action is considered neither rationally nor legally unless it is according to a law that is not contrary to reason and proof. As for consultative judgments, it is said that laws are prescribed from the people of the Sharia except in matters of obstinacy and jihad, contrary to contentious judgments; and not everyone has insight into every judgment, but some judgments are specific to certain individuals as previously mentioned. In summary, it seems that one must direct effort to the appropriate industrial tool, which is what produces assent (tasdiq) insofar as it is a producer of assent through the syllogism with its major premise omitted; and this is called thinking (tafkir) or inner conscience (damir). You have known that thinking is part of the syllogism of that syllogism; if that syllogism is completed, it is considered probable that it is dialectical; because the dialectical is that which consists of genuinely praiseworthy premises, while the rhetorical is that which consists of praiseworthy premises according to outward opinion. Since the consideration of the dialectical syllogism, which is called logical for the art of logic, then the consideration of what resembles it for the art — if the consideration is of truth and what resembles truth — for one art; so the consideration of praiseworthy premises in truth and praiseworthy premises in opinion, and their use, is for one art just as the consideration of the true. And truth from which demonstration emanates, and the praiseworthy from which dialectic emanates, are for one art if the logical art in primary merit is demonstration; and dialectic is similar to it when the praiseworthy premises resemble truth. Human instincts are passionate about seeking truth, but the path to it is difficult; some are guided to it, and some fall into what resembles it.

Chapter on the participations of rhetoric with other arts and its differences from them.
Indeed the art of rhetoric is of great benefit; that is because truthful judgments in matters of justice and goodness are more beneficial and more widespread among people than their opposites; this is because the human species is intertwined through partnership, and partnership necessitates interaction and coexistence; interaction and coexistence necessitate truthful judgments in scientific matters that organize the common interest; these judgments need to be settled in souls and firmly established in beliefs. We have shown that demonstration is of little use in leading the masses to true belief, and we have shown that rhetoric is the one that undertakes this; thus one of the virtues of this art is its role in establishing these attributes in souls. Also, in particular matters, there are judgments required by correct reasoning; correct reasoning is not based on address and dialogue but its law is reflection and consideration, just as demonstration in universal theoretical matters is based on truth without dialogue. Then, what is established by demonstration from those matters, if one wishes to establish it in a soul that has descended from the rank of demonstration, dialectic is the most helpful thing in establishing it; similarly, what is grasped by correct reasoning, if one wishes to establish it in a soul that is weak in reasoning by itself, rhetoric is the most helpful thing in it. And if the manager of the people is not entitled to address them with correct scientific discourse regarding what should be believed, or with rational discourse regarding what should be done: then if we have a rhetorical ability that enables us to persuade the interlocutor with what he praises and opines, accepts, and matches his capacity and resembles him, then the art of rhetoric is one of the arts by which one is persuaded concerning opposites, just as the art of dialectic was an art by which one measures concerning opposites. But not that rhetoric persuades at one time that this very thing was and that it was not, nor that...
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage is a comparative epistemological discussion of rhetoric (khataba) relative to dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan). The author begins by noting that each art has multiple methods, but rhetoric does not have the power to persuade in all matters; rather, it works through the inner conscience (damir) and what embellishes it. The proof's importance is tied to the foundation of action, which must conform to reason and divine law. He then distinguishes consultative judgments (ahkam tashawuriyya) from contentious ones, noting that some judgments are specific to certain individuals. 

The author then turns to the core distinction: the art of logic primarily deals with demonstration, but there are approximations. Dialectic uses genuinely praiseworthy premises (mamdudat haqiqiyya), while rhetoric uses premises that are praiseworthy according to outward opinion (mamdudat bihasb badi' al-ra'y). Thinking (tafkir) is a part of a syllogism whose major premise is omitted, and this syllogism is considered probable and resembles dialectic if based on genuine praiseworthiness. The consideration of truth and its resemblance belongs to one art, just as the consideration of the true and the praiseworthy in opinion is for one art. 

He highlights that human nature is eager for truth but the path is difficult; some find it, others fall into its resemblance. Then in the new chapter section, he explains the great benefit of rhetoric: truthful judgments are more beneficial and widespread because humans are social, needing interaction and coexistence which require truthful judgments in scientific matters. These judgments must be firmly established in souls. Demonstration is ineffective for the masses, so rhetoric undertakes this task. Similarly, for particular matters requiring correct reasoning (ta'aqqul), rhetoric is most helpful for those weak in reasoning. Finally, rhetoric can be used to persuade concerning opposites, like dialectic, but not to assert contradictory propositions simultaneously. This passage clarifies the epistemological role of rhetoric as a necessary tool for disseminating truth among those incapable of demonstration.

Key Terms
الخطابة
البرهان
الجدل
الجمهور

Key Takeaway

Classifying Rhetorical Beliefs and the Three Persuasive Pillars
Author classifies rhetorical beliefs and describes three persuasive pillars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify rhetorical beliefs as artificial or non-artificial and identify the three pillars of rhetorical persuasion from a given example.

Core Idea
Rhetoric has two sources: skill (artificial) and raw situation; its persuasive power rests on three pillars.

This passage introduces the classification of rhetorical beliefs (tasdiqat khatabiyya) into artificial and non-artificial categories. It then explains how artificial beliefs are generated through topics and types, and finally lists the three pillars that make speech persuasive: establishment (tathbit), speaker's demeanor, and gradual enticement (istidraj). Understanding this structure helps you analyze how Islamic orators construct convincing arguments.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish artificial from non-artificial
Identify the two main categories: artificial beliefs (sina'iyya) come from the orator's skill, while non-artificial ones arise from the situation itself, not from crafted speech.
The author says non-artificial beliefs are 'not like issues'—they are simply present.
2
Examine the two types of artificial beliefs
Some artificial beliefs are prepared from pre-existing premises called 'topics' (mawadi'), which are parts of syllogisms potentially or actually. Others come from rules ('types', anwa') that allow you to generate premises on the spot during debate.
Topics are ready-made; types are generative principles.
3
Work through the example
The example shows shifting judgment from an enemy to a friend using a rule: if Zayd (enemy) deserves mistreatment, then 'Amr (friend) deserves kindness. This illustrates applying a type to produce a persuasive premise.
This demonstrates how rules help tailor arguments to the audience.
4
Identify the three pillars of artificial persuasion
The author lists three: (1) tathbit (establishing the claim), (2) the speaker's manner (kayfiyya), and (3) istidraj (gradually drawing the audience). The first pillar may aim directly at the goal or at a supporting point.
These pillars are the core tools of rhetorical technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم التصديقات الخطابية قد تكون صناعية، وقد لا تكون؛ وهذه لا تكون بمثله مسائل لوجود الأمر الذي يدعو إليه. وأما الصناعية فإنها إما تكون من فكرتنا وباختيالنا فنحن نستنبط المواضع والأنواع الخطابية، ونعلم ترتيب القياس الخطابي وما يتعلق به هذا من جهة الأصل. فأما من جهة الاستعمال فبعضها قد تكون معدة لنا من قبل وهي المقدمات التي تسمى في هذا الكتاب مواضع ؛ وهي المقدمات التي من بيانها أجزاء قياس بالقوة أو بالفعل، وبعضها لا تكون معدة بل المعد فيها أصول وقوانين إذا علمناها استخرجنا منها وقت المحاورة مقدمات خطابية. وتلك القوانين تسمّى في هذا الكتاب أنواعًا فلا تزال تتوصل من نتيجة مستمرة ) على طريق الاستدراج إلى حصول الغرض؛ (1) مثاله إذا كان قد تيسر لنا عند تحصيل هذه القوة كيف ينتقل الحكم من ضد إلى عدوك ضد على سبيل الإقناع. ثم خاطبنا مشيرين فعلنا إن كان زيد الذي هو استوجب إساءتك إليه فعمرو الذي هو صديقك استوجب إحسانك إليه؛ كنا قد استخرجنا هذا من قانون معد لنا. والتصديقات الصناعية التي يختال لها بالكلام، ويكون ذلك الكلام مقنعاً لوضع أو شرع هي ثلاثة أصناف: [1] أحدها العمود الذي يسمى تثبيتا في هذا الكتاب. [ب] والثاني في كيفية المتكلم تأديته الكلام في سمته. [ت] والثالث استدراج السامعين؛ وهذا الذي هو عمود وتثبيت فإنه قد يكون نحو الغرض نفسه، وقد يكون نحو تقرير شيء
Then the rhetorical beliefs may be artificial (sina'iyya) or not; and these are not like issues for the existence of the matter that calls for it. As for the artificial, they are either from our thought and choice, so we deduce the topics (mawadi') and types (anwa') of rhetoric, and we know the arrangement of the rhetorical syllogism and what pertains to it, this from the perspective of principle. As for from the perspective of use, some of them may be prepared for us beforehand, and they are the premises that are called in this book 'topics'; they are the premises from whose clarification [are] parts of a syllogism potentially or actually. And some of them are not prepared, but what is prepared in them are principles and rules; if we know them, we extract from them at the time of debate rhetorical premises. And those rules are called in this book 'types'; so you continually reach from a continuous result in the way of gradual enticement to attainment of the goal. An example: if it is facilitated for us when acquiring this faculty, how does the judgment transfer from a contrary to your enemy contrary in the way of persuasion. Then we addressed gestures—we did if Zayd was the one who deserved your mistreatment, then 'Amr, who is your friend, deserved your kindness; we would have extracted this from a rule prepared for us. And the artificial beliefs that one chooses for speech, and that speech is persuasive by situation or law, are three kinds: [1] one of them is the pillar that is called 'establishment' (tathbit) in this book. [2] The second concerns the manner of the speaker delivering his speech in his demeanor. [3] The third is drawing the audience; and this which is the pillar and establishment may be towards the goal itself, or towards establishing something.
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage classifies rhetorical beliefs (tasdiqat khatabiyya) into artificial (sina'iyya) and non-artificial. The artificial ones are derived either from our own deduction of topics (mawadi') and types (anwa'), or from pre-prepared premises called 'topics' that are potential parts of a syllogism, or from general rules called 'types' that allow us to generate premises on the spot. An example illustrates: if the audience is hostile to an enemy, you can shift the judgment to a friend using a rule. The author then lists three kinds of artificial persuasion that make speech persuasive: (1) the pillar called tathbit (establishment), which may aim directly at the goal or at establishing a supporting point; (2) the speaker's manner (kayfiyya) in delivering speech; (3) enticement (istidraj) of the audience. These are the main tools of rhetorical persuasion.

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical beliefs are either artificial (crafted through topics and types) or non-artificial, and artificial persuasion rests on three pillars: establishment, speaker's manner, and gradual enticement.

Three Types of Rhetorical Disputation and Their Listeners
Author analyzes rhetorical disputation and categorizes discussions and listeners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of rhetorical disputation (consultative, contentious, disputative) and identify the corresponding listener roles.

Core Idea
Rhetoric is not just persuasion but a structured disputation classified by time and subject.

In this passage, the author moves beyond a simple view of rhetoric as mere persuasion. He dissects rhetorical disputation (munāzaʿa) into three distinct types based on whether the subject is future or past good/evil, and whether the discussion involves consultation, praise/blame, or complaint/excuse. He also identifies three listener roles. Understanding this classification helps you see how rhetoric functions in real Islamic discourse, from political consultation to judicial debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of classification
Notice that the author divides rhetorical discussions first by whether the subject is future or occurring (past/present). This temporal distinction is key.
2
Distinguish the three discussion types
For future matters, it is consultative (mushāwariyya). For existing good/evil, it is contentious (munāfiriyya). For past good/evil, it is disputative (mushājariyya).
3
Identify the three listener roles
The listeners are opponent (khaṣm), judge (ḥākim), and spectators (nuẓẓār). Each role interacts differently with the disputation.
4
Match the judge's role to time
The judge in future matters is a leader managing the community's affair; in occurring matters, he is a trusted mediator investigating the situation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: فيما يتعلّق به الخطابة من الأعراض الأولية وغير ذلك فنقول: إن المنازعة في كون الشيء ولا لكونه منازعة عامة لجميع الأنواع الخطابية؛ فإذا رجع إلى التفصيل والتخصيص فأكثر أصناف المحاورات العامية في الأمور الجزئية يرجع إلى ما فيه خير أو شر. والجزئيات إما مستقبلة وإما واقعة؛ ويبعد أن يقع للجمهور منازعة في جزئي مستقبل واقع بالطبع والاتفاق هل هو خير أو شر؛ فإن هذا النحو من النظر بأهل العلوم أولى بل إن تنازعوا في هذا تنازعوا. وهم يشيرون بالتحرز عنه إن كان شرا، أو التوقع إن كان خيرا. وأما الأمور الواقعة من الخير والشر فإما أن يراد إثبات وجود هذا الخير للإنسان، أو هذا الشر له فقط؛ وهذه هي المفاوضة التي يمدح فيها أو يدم. ولا كلام فيه بل الكلام في المعدوم من الخير والشر مما نقل؛ وإنما يمدح ويذم في أكثر الأمر ما هو موجود خاص فيكون أولى لأزمنة لموضوعات هذه المفاوضة هو الزمان الحاضر. وأما أن وجود هذا أو ذلك من إنسان آخر بإرادته؛ وحينئذ إما أن يكون للخطيب تفاوض إنسانًا في أن خيرًا وصل إليه منه، أو من إنسان آخر. وليس مع الاعتراف تشاجر وتنازع البتة؛ فقصارى ذلك محاورة في الشكر. وإن كان هناك تنازع فذلك إما من باب المدح والذم، وإما من باب الإثبات والإبطال؛ وهذه منازعة عامة. فإذا جعل بدل الخير شر كانت للمفاوضة جارية على سبيل شكاية واعتذار؛ وذلك في أمر ماض لا محالة. فإذن المفاوضات الخطابية ثلاثة: مشاورية، ومنافرية، ومشاجرية؛ وكذلك السامعون ثلاثة: خصم، وحاكم بإقناع أحدهما، ونظار. أما الحاكم في الأمور المستقبلة فهو الرئيس المدبر لأمر الجماعة، وفي الواقعة يكون كالمتوسط الموثوق تفحصه. وأما النظار
Chapter: On what pertains to rhetoric from primary accidents and other matters. We say: Disputation about whether a thing exists or does not exist is a general disputation for all rhetorical types. When it comes to detail and specification, most types of common dialogues concerning particular matters return to what involves good or evil. Particulars are either future or occurring. It is unlikely that the public would dispute about a future particular that occurs by nature and coincidence whether it is good or evil; for this kind of examination is more appropriate to people of knowledge, but if they do dispute about it, they dispute. They indicate caution if it is evil, or anticipation if it is good. As for occurring matters of good and evil, it is either intended to prove the existence of this good for a person, or this evil for him only; this is the discussion in which one praises or blames. There is no talk about it, but talk is about the non-existent good and evil that is transmitted. Indeed, praise and blame in most cases are for what is specifically existing, so the time most appropriate for the subjects of this discussion is the present time. As for the existence of this or that from another person by his will; then either the orator discusses with a person that a good reached him from him, or from another person. With acknowledgment, there is no quarrel or dispute at all; the utmost of that is a dialogue of thanks. If there is dispute, then that is either from the domain of praise and blame, or from the domain of affirmation and negation; this is a general disputation. If good is replaced by evil, then the discussion proceeds as a complaint and excuse; that is necessarily about a past matter. Therefore, rhetorical discussions are three: consultative, contentious, and disputative. Likewise, the listeners are three: opponent, judge who persuades one of them, and spectators. As for the judge in future matters, he is the leader who manages the community's affair, and in occurring matters he is like a trusted mediator in his investigation. As for the spectators.
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of rhetoric (الخطابة), focusing on the types of disputation (منازعة) involved. The author opens by stating that disputation about existence is general to all rhetorical types. Then he narrows to particulars involving good and evil, dividing them into future and occurring. Future particulars are rarely disputed by the public, but if they are, it involves caution or anticipation. Occurring matters of good and evil lead to praise or blame. The author distinguishes between discussing existing goods/evils (praise/blame) and non-existent ones (transmitted). The present time is most relevant. Then he considers when good or evil comes from another's will, leading to thanks or, if disputed, to praise/blame or affirmation/negation. When good is replaced by evil, the discussion becomes complaint or excuse about the past. The author concludes by categorizing rhetorical discussions into three: consultative (مشاورية), contentious (منافرية), and disputative (مشاجرية). Similarly, the listeners are three: opponent (خصم), judge (حاكم), and spectators (نظار). The judge's role differs for future matters (leader) vs. occurring matters (investigator). The passage ends mid-sentence discussing spectators.

Key Terms

منازعة
مشاورية
منافرية
مشاجرية

B) Munāfiriyya
C) Mushājariyya

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical disputation is systematically classified by time (future vs. past) and subject (good/evil), with corresponding listener roles.

Virtues and Vices in Rhetorical Praise and Blame
Author defines virtues and vices for rhetorical praise and blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight parts of virtue and distinguish each from its opposite vice as defined for rhetorical persuasion in Islamic contexts.

Core Idea
Virtues in Islamic rhetoric are defined not by abstract ethics but by their relation to Sharia and their effect on the orator's credibility.

In this passage, the author explains how knowledge of virtues and vices serves the orator (al-khatib) in the art of praise and blame. These are not merely ethical categories but rhetorical tools: by manifesting virtue, the orator gains trust; by blaming vice, he discredits opponents. The definitions given are functional—they describe what a virtuous person does, especially in relation to Sharia, rather than providing essential definitions. This lesson will guide you through these definitions and their rhetorical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rhetorical purpose
Notice how the author begins by stating that virtues and vices are useful in praise and blame, and also in preparing the orator for acceptance of his words. This frames the definitions as practical tools.
2
Extract the general definition of virtue
Find the sentence that defines virtue (al-fadila) as a power or faculty of good deliberation to acquire what is good or considered good, and that it performs and preserves great religious matters. This is the genus for all specific virtues.
Virtue is a type of the beautiful (al-jamil), highlighting its desirability.
3
List the eight parts of virtue
The author enumerates: righteousness, courage, manliness, high ambition, generosity, forbearance, gentleness, and wisdom. Write them down and note that each has an opposite vice listed later.
Some virtues are transitive (benefiting others) like righteousness and courage.
4
Analyze each virtue's Sharia link
For each virtue, identify how it is tied to Sharia. For example, courage is defined by performing jihad as commanded, generosity by expending wealth within bounds, chastity by using appetitive power as permitted. This Islamicizes the Greek virtue list.
The author explicitly uses 'according to Sharia' or 'as commanded by Sharia' for several.
5
Recognize the descriptive nature of these definitions
The author calls these 'descriptions according to appearances' (rusum bi-hasab al-zawahir). They are not essential definitions (hadd) but practical identifications for rhetoric. This distinction is crucial for understanding their epistemological status.
The opposite vices are listed simply as counterparts, not defined in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في المنافريات وهو باب المدح والذم

وأما الأنواع النافعة في المدح والذم المتعلق بالفضيلة والرذيلة وما يجري مجراها فإنها مع أنها تنفع في المدح والذم فقد تنفع في إعداد الخطيب للتصديق بقوله وإن كان في غير باب المنافرة. وذلك لأنه إذا أبت فضيلة نفسه جعل نفسه أهلا لقبول الناس قوله، وكذلك إذا ذم خصمه عرضه لرد الناس قوله والممادح المنسوبة إلى أنها فضيلة، وما يتبع الفضيلة من الجمال والحسن وغير ذلك من الممادح التي قد يتعدى بمدحها الناس والملائكة إلى أشخاص أخر يمدح بها. فالجميل هو المختار لأجل نفسه، والمحمود الذي لا لأجل شيء آخر بل لأجل خيريته فإنه جميل من هذه الجهة. والفضيلة نوع من الجميل لأنها قوة أي ملكة حسن التأني لتحصيل ما هو خير أو يرى خيرًا وهي التي تفعل وتحفظ الأمور الشرعية العظيمة من كل جهة.

وأجزاء الفضيلة هي البر والشجاعة والمروّة وكبر الهمة والسخاء والحلم واللين والحكمة. فمن الفضائل لا محالة ما يتعدى خيره إلى غير الفاضل مثل البر والشجاعة والسخاوة؛ فالبر فضيلة عادلة مقدرة بحسب تقدير الشريعة لكل ما يستحقه، والشجاعة فضيلة يكون المرء بها فعالا أفعال الجهاد على ما تأمر به الشريعة نصرة لها. والسخاوة فضيلة يكون المرء بها فعالا للجميل ببذل المال والعفّة فضيلة يكون المرء بها في استعمال القوة الشهوانية البدنية على القدر الذي ترخض فيه الشريعة. والمروة فضيلة يعمل النبيل بالتوسع في الإطعام وصغر النفس. وكبر الهمة فضيلة يكون المرء بها فعالا لأفعال عظيمة المنزلة من الجهد واللين فضيلة في الرأي يكون المرء بها حسن التعقل والمشورة نحو الخيرات والجميل. وما يكون في المقابلة لكل واحد منها من الرذائل فهو الجور والجبن والدناءة والفجور والبذالة والسفالة والحماقة؛ وهذه رسوم بحسب الظواهر لهذه القضايا التي يمدح بها. ولا يبعد أن يصدر عن الشجاع فعل لا يصدر إلا عن شجاع أو يلحقه انفعال لا يكون إلا للشجاع، وكذلك قد يصدر عن السخي وغيره، ويلحقه أيضًا ما لا يكون إلا له. ثم الخطيب له أن يستعمل في الخطابة من الألفاظ ما يكون بطريق الاستعارة أو الكناية كما يقول للفسيق إنه لطيف العشرة يعني أنه حليم، وللغضوب القطوب إنه نبيل ذو سخت، وللأبله المغفل عن اللذات إنه سليم القلب. وله أن ينتقل من المدح إلى المشورة، ومن المشورة إلى المدح، وينبغي أن يؤكّد أمر المدح وأمر المشورة بألفاظ معظمة مفخمة كما يقال إنه فريد عصره وقدوة أهل زمانه مثلا. وفائدة الاطلاع على الفضائل والرذائل أن يكون طبعه مائلا إلى ما يكون مفيدًا من الأفعال ومتنفرًا عما يكون بخلافه، ويمكن أن يرتقي المرء بأفعاله إلى درجة هي وسيلة إلى السعادة. والسعادة قد تكون كسبية، وقد لا تكون. والسعادة على أربعة أقسام؛ دينية، ومالية، وبدنية، واتفاقية وهي السعادة المشهورة كما مر ذكرها من قبل. ثم من اللوازم أن يكون المرء متأملا في أفعاله فإن منها ما يلزمه أن يرتكب، ومنها ما يجب عليه أن يجتنب. وقد كان من الأعراض ما يفضي إلى الأول منهما عاليا نحو العفة ورعاية الغرض ومحبة أهل الصلاح مثلا، ومنها ما
Section: On Disputations (Munafirat), which is the chapter on praise and blame.

As for the beneficial types in praise and blame related to virtue and vice and what follows their course, they, while benefiting in praise and blame, may benefit in preparing the orator for the acceptance of his statement even if it is outside the chapter of disputation. That is because if he manifests his own virtue, he makes himself worthy of people accepting his statement, and similarly if he blames his opponent, he exposes him to people rejecting his statement. The praiseworthy qualities attributed to being virtues, and what follows virtue such as beauty and goodness, and other praiseworthy qualities that may extend by their praise to people and angels to other persons who are praised by them. The beautiful is that which is chosen for its own sake, and the praised is that which is not for something else but for its goodness; it is beautiful from this aspect. Virtue is a type of the beautiful because it is a power, i.e. a faculty of good deliberation to acquire what is good or considered good, and it is that which performs and preserves the great religious matters from every aspect.

The parts of virtue are: righteousness, courage, manliness, high ambition, generosity, forbearance, gentleness, and wisdom. Among the virtues, there are inevitably those whose good extends to others besides the virtuous, such as righteousness, courage, and generosity. Righteousness is a just virtue measured according to the estimation of the Sharia for everything it deserves. Courage is a virtue by which a person performs acts of jihad as commanded by the Sharia in support of it. Generosity is a virtue by which a person performs the beautiful by expending wealth. Chastity is a virtue by which a person is in using the bodily appetitive power to the extent permitted by the Sharia. Manliness is a virtue by which the nobleman acts with expansiveness in feeding and humility of self. High ambition is a virtue by which a person performs great deeds of high rank with effort. Gentleness is a virtue in opinion by which a person has good rationality and consultation towards good things and the beautiful. What is opposite to each of them from vices are: injustice, cowardice, baseness, lewdness, profligacy, lowness, and foolishness. These are descriptions according to appearances for these matters that are praised. It is not unlikely that from the courageous an action proceeds that does not proceed except from a courageous, or an affection occurs that is only for the courageous; similarly, it may proceed from the generous and others, and also occur what is only for him. Then the orator may use in rhetoric from words that are by way of metaphor or metonymy, such as saying of the wicked that he is gentle in company, meaning he is forbearing; of the angry, frowning one that he is noble, generous; of the stupid, heedless of pleasures that he is pure of heart. And he may transition from praise to counsel, and from counsel to praise, and he should emphasize the matter of praise and the matter of counsel with grandiose, exalting words, as it is said of him that he is the unique of his age and the model of his time, for example. The benefit of examining virtues and vices is that his nature inclines toward what is beneficial of actions and recoils from what is otherwise, and a person can elevate himself by his actions to a degree that is a means to happiness. Happiness may be acquired, and may not be. Happiness is of four divisions: religious, financial, physical, and coincidental, which is the well-known happiness as previously mentioned. Then it is necessary for a person to reflect on his actions, for among them are what he must commit, and among them are what he must avoid. And among the habits are what lead to the first of them, such as chastity, observing the purpose, and love of the righteous, for example, and among them what
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is from a section on rhetoric (al-khataba) within the five arts of logic. The author defines the key virtues and vices used in rhetorical praise and blame. He begins by explaining the purpose: orators use praise of virtues to make themselves credible and blame of vices to discredit opponents. He then defines virtue (al-fadila) as a power or faculty for good deliberation, and lists its eight parts: righteousness, courage, manliness, high ambition, generosity, forbearance, gentleness, and wisdom. Each virtue is defined in terms of its specific action and relation to Sharia. For example, courage (al-shaja'a) is performing jihad as commanded. The opposite vices are listed as counterparts. The author notes that these are descriptions according to appearances (rusum bi-hasab al-zawahir), meaning they are not essential definitions but practical identifications for rhetorical contexts. He then describes how the orator can use metaphor and metonymy to praise or blame indirectly, and can move between praise and counsel. Finally, the passage discusses the benefit of this knowledge: it inclines one's nature toward beneficial actions and away from harmful ones, leading to happiness (sa'ada), which is divided into four types: religious, financial, physical, and coincidental. The orator must reflect on actions—some must be performed, others avoided. The passage ends abruptly, but the main point is that rhetorical persuasion in praise and blame requires knowledge of these moral qualities.

The orator uses Sharia-tied definitions of virtues and vices to persuade; these definitions are practical descriptions, not metaphysical essences.

The Five Types of Non-Technical Assents in Disputation
Author enumerates five categories of non-technical assents used in disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five types of non-technical assents used in Islamic disputation.

Core Idea
Persuasion in disputes often relies on customs, testimonies, covenants, punishments, and oaths, not formal syllogisms.

In this passage, the author distinguishes assents produced by a technical art from those that are not. The latter are especially useful in disputes. Understanding these five categories gives you a practical toolkit for analyzing arguments in Islamic legal and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching distinction
Note the author's division: assents that come 'by art' (bi-sina'a) and those that are 'not from art' (laysat 'an sina'a). The latter are the focus.
2
Locate the list of five types
In the Arabic, find the five items enumerated with letters: [أ] السنن, [ب] الشهود, [ت] العهود, [ث] العقوبات, [ج] الأيمان.
The letters help memorize the sequence.
3
Understand each type with context
Customs (sunan) are established practices; testimonies (shuhud) are witness statements; covenants (uhud) are agreements; punishments (uqubat) are penal codes; oaths (ayman) are sworn statements.
Each plays a distinct role in disputation.
4
Relate each type to a real Islamic example
For example, in a fiqh dispute, one might cite a custom (e.g., 'the 'urf allows this transaction'), a testimony (e.g., 'two witnesses saw the contract'), a covenant (e.g., 'the treaty specifies this condition'), a punishment (e.g., 'the hadd for theft is amputation'), or an oath (e.g., 'the defendant swears innocence').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعتذر كما مرَّ فإنه بإزاء المتظلم، وكذلك المستغفر وهو الذي يلتمس الحلم أو التفضّل؛ وهذه كلها في التصديقات الواقعة بصناعة. وأما التي ليست عن صناعة وهي التي أكثر نفعها في المشاجرات فإنها على أقسام خمسة؛ [أ] السنن [ب] والشهود، [ت] والعهود، [ث] والعقوبات، [ج] والأيمان.
Then the apologist, as mentioned, is opposite the complainant, and likewise the seeker of forgiveness who seeks forbearance or favor; all these are among assents occurring by art. As for those not from art, and which are most beneficial in disputes, they are of five divisions: [a] customs, [b] testimonies, [t] covenants, [th] punishments, [j] oaths.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage defines a classification of non-technical assents. The author first distinguishes two contexts: assents that are produced by a technical art (such as logic or rhetoric) and those that are not. The latter are particularly useful in disputes (mushajarat). He then enumerates exactly five types: (1) customs (sunan) — established practices or laws, both written and unwritten; (2) testimonies (shuhud) — eyewitness accounts or evidence; (3) covenants (uhud) — pacts and agreements; (4) punishments (uqubat) — penal codes; and (5) oaths (ayman) — sworn statements. These categories serve as commonplaces for argumentation when one lacks a formal syllogism. For example, in a legal debate, one might appeal to a well-known custom (like the prohibition of intoxicants) or to an oath taken by a party. The classification is exhaustive for non-technical persuasive premises in the context of disputation.

Key Takeaway
Non-technical assents—customs, testimonies, covenants, punishments, and oaths—are foundational in Islamic disputation, providing premises that do not depend on formal logical training.

The Five Types of Non-Demonstrative Arguments in Rhetoric
Author lists and introduces the five rhetorical argument types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of rhetorical arguments (sunan, shuhud, uhud, uqubat, ayman) and explain how they differ from demonstrative proofs.

Core Idea
Rhetoric relies on five non-demonstrative arguments, not logical certainty.

In this passage, the author introduces the five categories of persuasive arguments used in rhetoric. These arguments are not based on formal logic but serve the orator in disputations. Understanding this classification helps distinguish rhetorical persuasion from demonstrative proof.

Guided Path
1
List the five types
Find the Arabic list: السنن, الشهود, العهود, العقوبات, الأيمان. Write them in order as given.
The author uses bracketed letters A, B, T, Th, J to enumerate.
2
Note their nature
The passage says these are 'ليست عن صناعة' (not from an art) and their benefit is in 'المشاجرات' (disputations). This means they are non-demonstrative and used for persuasion, not certainty.
3
Understand the context
Rhetoric (khataba) is one of the five logical arts. These five types are tools for the orator (khatib) to convince an audience.
4
Compare with demonstrative proof
Unlike syllogisms that yield certain conclusions, these arguments are probabilistic or persuasive. The judge (hakim) must evaluate them critically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التي ليست عن صناعة وهي التي أكثر نفعها في المشاجرات فإنها على أقسام خمسة؛ [ أ ] السنن [ب] والشهود، [ت] والعهود، [ث] والعقوبات، [ج] والأيمان. أما السنن المكتوبة فربَّما اقتصر الخطيب إلى مناقضة موجبها فيجد إلى إظهار يقتضي البعض سبيلا بإظهار ما يظهر منه التبديل والتغيير. وإن غير المكتوبة مأمون التغيير لأن أهل المروة لا ينافسون غير السنة المكتوبة بل ينحرفون إلى مقتضى السنة المكتوبة المشتركة؛ وذلك بحسب ما يقول لأن المكتوبة إنما احتيج إليها لعجز الجمهور عن تقدير الغير المكتوبة وتفضيلها. وللغافل أن يتعقل في تفضيل المشتركة فإن فيه كفاية. وكان له أن يخصص المكتوبة بحكم العقد. ثمَّ نقول والحاكم الفاضل هو بمنزلة النار المخلصة بعض الجواهر عن بعض فيلزمه أن يهتدي لهذا التخليص، وينظر في واجب الأمر والحاكم البصير ربَّما رأى أن يرجح حجة العقل، وربَّما رأى أن يرجح هو الحكم. وإذا أشكلت عليه المصلحة اعتصم بالتوقف أي أن يطلع على الصواب؛ فهذا وأمثاله مما يقوله الخطيب حين أشهد له السنة المشتركة، فإن لم توافقه وكانت المكتوبة أوقف له كان له أن يعتبر المكتوبة.
As for that which is not from an art, and which has most of its benefit in disputations, it is divided into five divisions: [A] the customs, [B] the witnesses, [T] the covenants, [Th] the punishments, [J] the oaths. As for the written customs, perhaps the orator confines himself to contradicting their implication, finding a way to show something that appears to indicate alteration and change. And the unwritten is secure from change because the people of manliness do not compete with the unwritten custom but rather incline to the requirement of the common written custom; and that is according to what he says because the written was only needed due to the public's inability to appreciate the unwritten and prefer it. And the heedless person can ponder the preference of the common, for in it is sufficiency. And he may particularize the written by the rule of the contract. Then we say: the virtuous judge is like fire that purifies some substances from others, so it is necessary that he be guided to this purification, and consider the obligatory matter. And the insightful judge may sometimes see to prefer the argument of reason, and sometimes see to prefer his judgment. And when the benefit is problematic for him, he clings to suspension, i.e., until he ascertains the correct. This and its likes are what the orator says when the common custom testifies for him; but if it does not agree with him and the written is favorable to him, he may consider the written.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage defines the five types of non-demonstrative arguments used in rhetoric (khataba) — which is one of the five logical arts. These arguments are based on customs (sunan), witnesses (shuhud), covenants (uhud), punishments (uqubat), and oaths (ayman). The author then focuses on the first type, sunan (customs or laws), distinguishing between written and unwritten ones. The rhetorician (khatib) may exploit ambiguities in written law to argue for alteration, while unwritten customs are more stable because people of honor adhere to them. The judge is compared to a refiner of metals: he must extract the truth. If the common custom (sunna mushtaraka) supports his case, he uses it; otherwise, he may fall back on written law. The passage emphasizes the flexibility of rhetorical argumentation in contrast to demonstrative proof.

الشهود
العهود
العقوبات
الأيمان

Sunan: Written and Unwritten Customs in Rhetoric
Author examines the first type (sunan) in detail, distinguishing written and unwritten.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between written and unwritten sunan and explain how a khatib or hakim would employ them in argumentation.

Core Idea
Unwritten customs are more stable than written law in rhetorical argument.

After introducing the five types, the author focuses on the first type, sunan (customs/laws). He divides them into written and unwritten, and discusses how the orator and judge use them. This reveals the flexibility and persuasive power of custom-based arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions of sunna
The passage mentions 'السنن المكتوبة' (written customs) and 'غير المكتوبة' (unwritten customs). Write them down.
2
Understand the orator's strategy
The orator may contradict the implication of written law to show alteration. He finds a way to argue for change.
The text says: 'ربَّما اقتصر الخطيب إلى مناقضة موجبها فيجد إلى إظهار يقتضي البعض سبيلا بإظهار ما يظهر منه التبديل والتغيير.'
3
Analyze the stability of unwritten custom
Unwritten customs are secure from change because 'أهل المروة' (people of manliness) do not compete with them but incline to the common written custom.
This suggests social pressure preserves unwritten norms.
4
Role of the virtuous judge (hakim)
The judge is like fire that purifies metal. He must discern truth and balance between written law, reason, and common custom. When uncertain, he suspends judgment.
The analogy: 'الحاكم الفاضل هو بمنزلة النار المخلصة بعض الجواهر عن بعض'.

Source Text

As for that which is not from an art, and which has most of its benefit in disputations, it is divided into five divisions: [A] the customs, [B] the witnesses, [T] the covenants, [Th] the punishments, [J] the oaths. As for the written customs, perhaps the orator confines himself to contradicting their implication, finding a way to show something that appears to indicate alteration and change. And the unwritten is secure from change because the people of manliness do not compete with the unwritten custom but rather incline to the requirement of the common written custom; and that is according to what he says because the written was only needed due to the public's inability to appreciate the unwritten and prefer it. And the heedless person can ponder the preference of the common, for in it is sufficiency. And he may particularize the written by the rule of the contract. Then we say: the virtuous judge is like fire that purifies some substances from others, so it is necessary that he be guided to this purification, and consider the obligatory matter. And the insightful judge may sometimes see to prefer the argument of reason, and sometimes see to prefer his judgment. And when the benefit is problematic for him, he clings to suspension, i.e., until he ascertains the correct. Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage defines the five types of non-demonstrative arguments used in rhetoric (khataba) — which is one of the five logical arts. These arguments are based on customs (sunan), witnesses (shuhud), covenants (uhud), punishments (uqubat), and oaths (ayman). The author then focuses on the first type, sunan (customs or laws), distinguishing between written and unwritten ones. The rhetorician (khatib) may exploit ambiguities in written law to argue for alteration, while unwritten customs are more stable because people of honor adhere to them. The judge is compared to a refiner of metals: he must extract the truth. If the common custom (sunna mushtaraka) supports his case, he uses it; otherwise, he may fall back on written law. The passage emphasizes the flexibility of rhetorical argumentation in contrast to demonstrative proof.

Key Terms
السنة المكتوبة
السنة غير المكتوبة
السنة المشتركة
الحاكم الفاضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the unwritten custom considered secure from change?
A) Because it is written in sacred texts.
B) Because people of manliness do not compete with it but incline to the common written custom.
C) Because the judge always prefers it.
D) Because it is easier to manipulate.

Key Takeaway
The stability of unwritten custom makes it a powerful rhetorical tool for the orator and judge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In a dispute about a marriage contract, a judge must choose between a written requirement (witnesses) and the common custom (family agreement). Using the passage, explain which the judge should prefer and why.

The Three Common Rhetorical Matters
Classification of three common rhetorical types and their primary aims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the three common rhetorical types and match them to their primary rhetorical aim (praise, consultation, or equality).

Core Idea
Three rhetorical types, each specialized for a different aim: possibility for advice, existence for justice, magnification for praise.

In this passage, the author presents a concise classification of the three rhetorical matters that are common across all rhetorical situations. You will learn to distinguish statements about what is possible vs. impossible, what exists vs. does not exist, and magnification vs. minimization, and understand which aim each serves best. This classification is foundational for analyzing how arguments are framed in Islamic oratory, legal reasoning, and debate.

Guided Path
1
List the three common types
Locate the three types mentioned in the Arabic: 'القول في الممكن وغير الممكن' (the possible and impossible), 'القول في الكائن وغير الكائن' (the existent and non-existent), and 'القول في التكبير والتصغير' (magnification and minimization). Note the Arabic terms as they appear.
These are the 'common types' (الأنواع المشتركة) shared among the three rhetorical arts.
2
Identify each type's primary aim
Read the author's assignments: magnification/minimization is most specific to praise (المدح), statements about existence/non-existence are most specific to equality or comparison (المساواة), and statements about possibility/impossibility are most specific to consultation (المشورة). Understand that these are primary associations, not exclusive uses.
The author uses أخص (more specific) to indicate the primary domain.
3
Connect types to Islamic sciences
Relate each type to a real Islamic practice: a mufti giving a fatwa often discusses what is possible or impossible according to Sharia (الممكن شرعاً); a judge in court examines evidence to determine what actually exists or not (الكائن وغير الكائن); a preacher or poet praises or blames through magnification or minimization (التكبير والتصغير).
This bridges rhetorical theory with practical examples from Fiqh, Qada, and Khutbah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في الأنواع المشتركة للأمور الخطابية ] وأما الأنواع المشتركة للأمور الخطابية الثلاثة؛ [1] كالقول في الممكن وغير الممكن [ب] والقول في الكائن وغير الكائن [ت] والقول في التكبير والتصغير. وإن كانت عامة للثلاثة فيُشبه أن يكون التكبير والتحقير أخص بالمدح. وأما الكائن وغير الكائن فإنه أخص بالمساواة. وأما الممكن وغير الممكن فإنه أخص بالمشورة.
Chapter: On the common types of rhetorical matters. As for the common types of the three rhetorical matters: [1] the statement about the possible and impossible, [b] the statement about the existent and non-existent, [t] the statement about magnification and minimization. And if they are general for the three, it seems that magnification and degradation are more specific to praise. As for the existent and non-existent, it is more specific to equality. As for the possible and impossible, it is more specific to consultation.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the common types of rhetorical matters. It begins by stating that there are three common types: statements about the possible/impossible, about the existent/non-existent, and about magnification/minimization. The author then notes that although these types are common to all rhetorical aims, each has a primary association: magnification/minimization is most closely tied to praise or blame; statements about existence/non-existence relate to equality or comparison; and statements about possibility/impossibility are most relevant to consultation or advice. This classification aligns with the three aims of rhetoric: deliberative (advisory), demonstrative (praise/blame), and judicial (equality/justice). For example, in Islamic legal reasoning, a mufti giving a fatwa often uses the possible/impossible ('هذا ممكن شرعاً') while a judge assesses existence/non-existence of evidence. The author sets the stage for detailed rules for each type.

Key Terms

الكائن وغير الكائن
التكبير والتصغير
المدح
المساواة
المشورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Magnification and minimization
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The three common rhetorical matters — possible/impossible, existent/non-existent, and magnification/minimization — are each primarily associated with one of the three rhetorical aims: consultation, justice, and praise, respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

For each, name the type and the associated aim.

Common Assents: Example and Thinking
Author introduces two types of common assents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of common assents (al-mithal and al-tafkir) and identify their role in rhetorical persuasion.

Core Idea
Common assents are the rhetorical bridge between mere opinion and conviction, using example and thinking.

In rhetoric, not every argument is a strict syllogism. The author now turns to 'common assents' (al-tasdiqat al-mushtaraka) – shared types of affirmations that persuade audiences. He names two: example (al-mithal) and thinking (al-tafkir). Understanding these helps us see how Islamic preachers and debaters used parables and reasoning to win hearts and minds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the new category
Find where the author signals a new topic: 'فلنفصل الأمر في التصديقات المشتركة' – 'let us detail the common assents.' This phrase marks the transition.
The word 'المشتركة' means shared, indicating these assents are common across many rhetorical contexts.
2
Extract the two types
The text states: 'وهي جنسان المثال والتفكير' – 'they are two types: example and thinking.' List these two species.
Note that 'جنسان' is dual, confirming exactly two.
3
Contrast with previous section
Recall that earlier the author covered magnification (تعظيم) and degradation (تحقير) in consultative matters. Common assents are a different rhetorical domain, not about emotional amplification but about producing assent.
This shows the author is systematically covering all rhetorical tools.
4
Define each component
From the context and key terms, define: 'المثال' – example, a rhetorical argument using a parallel case (like a parable); 'التفكير' – thinking, reasoning that leads to opinion. These are not strict logical proofs but persuasive arguments.
In Islamic sermons, Quranic parables (أمثال) are classic examples of mithal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أنواع التعظيم والتحقير فقد يكتفي فيه بما ذكر في المشوريات؛ وخصوصا بحسب أمر أمر من الأمور الجزئية فلنفصل الأمر في التصديقات المشتركة وهي جنسان المثال والتفكير.
As for the types of magnification and degradation, one may be satisfied with what was mentioned in the consultative matters; especially according to each particular matter. So let us detail the matter in the common assents, which are two types: example and thinking.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This short passage transitions from the discussion of the three common types to a new topic: the common assents (تصديقات مشتركة). The author says that regarding magnification and degradation (التعظيم والتحقير), one can rely on the rules already given in the section on consultative matters (المشوريات), particularly when applied to individual cases. Then he announces that he will now detail the common assents, which are of two types: example (مثال) and thinking (تفكير). 'Example' (مثال) here likely refers to rhetorical example (paradigm) as a form of argument, not the logical example in induction. 'Thinking' (تفكير) is close to 'opinion' or reasoning. This sets up the next discussion on how these assents produce conviction. In the Islamic context, example is used in sermons (as in the Quranic parables), and thinking refers to rational inference that leads to assent.

Key Terms
التصديقات المشتركة
المثال
التفكير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The common assents — example and thinking — are the two rhetorical pathways to persuade an audience without strict syllogistic proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the Quranic verse about the spider's web (Quran 29:41). Explain why this is an example (مثال) and how it persuades the audience about the weakness of idolatry.
?

Defining Praiseworthy Opinion
Author defines praiseworthy opinion and contrasts it with thinking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define praiseworthy opinion (al-ra'y al-mahmud) and distinguish it from thinking by its characteristics as stated in the passage.

Core Idea
Praiseworthy opinion is universal and practical, but only thinking with cause produces assent.

In this passage, the author defines praiseworthy opinion as a key component of the materials of thinking. Understanding this definition is crucial because it sets the foundation for distinguishing between mere opinion and genuinely persuasive reasoning. We will extract the defining features from the Arabic text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'al-ra'y al-mahmud' (praiseworthy opinion). Note that it is introduced as 'included in the materials of thinking'.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'mawadd al-tafkir' (materials of thinking). This places praiseworthy opinion within a broader category of cognitive content.
3
Extract the differentia
The text says it is a 'universal proposition' (qadiyya kulliya) concerning 'matters of knowledge from the aspect of what is effective or avoided'. This practical, universal nature distinguishes it from particular opinions or mere speculation.
The differentia includes both universality and practical orientation.
4
Compare with thinking
Thinking is described as 'an opinion close to the conclusion' and 'more apt to produce assent'. Contrast: opinion alone does not produce assent; it needs to be part of thinking with its cause.
This comparison sharpens the definition of praiseworthy opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرأي المحمود فهو داخل في مواد التفكير؛ والتفكير أولى أن يوقع التصديق. وأما الرأي فإنه قضية كلية لا جزئية وهي في أمور علمية، ومن جهة ما يؤثر أو يجتنب والتفكير رأي قريب من النتيجة؛ ونتائج الآراء إذا أخذت بانفرادها هي أيضًا آراء كما أن مقدماتها آراء لكنها إنما تكون تفكيرًا إقناعيًا إذا قرنت به العلة كما في قولنا إن معرفة الأحداث بالحكمة فضول فهو رأي، ونتيجة رأي هو أنهم حينئذ يكونون مدخرين ما لا ينتفعون به. والرأي الذي هو نتيجة إذا أخذت وحده لم ينتفع به؛ إذ ليس مقبولا بنفسه؛ إذ القبول بعد قبول مقدمة هي علة والرأي قد يحتاج إلى أن يقرن بكلام آخر، وقد لا يحتاج لظهوره عند المخاطب مثلا، وينبغي أن تكون الآراء الخطابية آراء مختارة ومقبولة عند إنسان إنسان ممن يخاطبه أو عند الأئمة.
As for praiseworthy opinion, it is included in the materials of thinking. And thinking is more apt to produce assent. As for opinion, it is a universal proposition, not particular, and it concerns matters of knowledge, from the aspect of what is effective or avoided. Thinking is an opinion close to the conclusion. The results of opinions, if taken individually, are also opinions just as their premises are opinions. But they only become persuasive thinking if the cause is attached to it, as in our saying: 'Knowledge of events through wisdom is superfluous' – that is an opinion, and the result of the opinion is that they are then storing what they do not benefit from. An opinion that is a result, if taken alone, is not beneficial, since it is not acceptable by itself; acceptance is after accepting a premise that is the cause. Opinion may need to be joined with another statement, or may not need it because of its clarity to the addressee, for example. And it is proper that the rhetorical opinions be chosen opinions, acceptable to each individual whom one addresses, or to the authorities.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage defines 'praiseworthy opinion' (الرأي المحمود) as part of the material of thinking (تفكير). The author states that thinking is more likely to produce assent than opinion alone. Opinion is defined as a universal proposition (قضية كلية) concerning matters of knowledge from a practical perspective (what to do or avoid). Thinking is described as an opinion that is close to the conclusion—i.e., a premise that leads directly to the desired conclusion. The author gives an example: 'Knowledge of events through wisdom is superfluous'—this is an opinion (premise). Its result (conclusion) is that those who do so store what they do not benefit from. But a conclusion taken alone is not persuasive; it must be attached to its cause (العلة), i.e., the reasoning. Thus, for a rhetorical argument to be persuasive, the cause must be stated. However, sometimes if the conclusion is obvious, it does not need the cause. The author emphasizes that rhetorical opinions should be chosen and acceptable to the specific audience or to the authorities (imams). This reflects the principle of tailoring arguments in Islamic da'wa and debate: one must use premises that the audience already accepts. For example, when debating a Mu'tazili, one uses premises they accept; with a layperson, commonsense examples.

Key Terms
الرأي المحمود
التفكير الإقناعي
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a praiseworthy opinion from other types of opinion?
A) It is always particular.
B) It is a universal proposition concerning practical matters.
C) It is always attached to its cause.
D) It is accepted by the authorities.

Key Takeaway
Praiseworthy opinion is defined as a universal, practically oriented proposition that becomes persuasive only when attached to its cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the genus and differentia in the definition of 'illah used in Hanafi usul: 'A description that is suitable for the ruling and is constant in presence and absence.' What genus and differentia can you extract?

Persuasive Thinking: The Role of Cause
Author explains the condition for persuasive thinking using an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an opinion becomes persuasive thinking and apply the condition of attaching the cause to an Islamic argument.

Core Idea
Persuasive thinking requires the cause; without it, a conclusion is not beneficial.

The passage explains that opinions and their results only become persuasive thinking (tafkir iqna'i) when the cause (illa) is attached. We will analyze the example given and learn to apply this condition to theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of persuasive thinking
Find where the author says opinions become persuasive thinking: 'they only become persuasive thinking if the cause is attached to it' (إنما تكون تفكيرًا إقناعيًا إذا قرنت به العلة).
2
Examine the example
The example: 'Knowledge of events through wisdom is superfluous' is an opinion. Its result (conclusion) is 'they are storing what they do not benefit from.' The cause (illa) is the reason why this knowledge is superfluous.
The cause is implicit: wasteful occupation with unnecessary detail.
3
Identify the missing cause
Without stating the cause, the conclusion alone is not persuasive. The cause bridges the premise and conclusion. In the example, the cause is that such knowledge leads to storing useless information.
The text says 'acceptance is after accepting a premise that is the cause.'
4
Apply to a new example
Take the argument: 'Wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating.' The opinion (premise) is 'Wine is intoxicating.' The cause (illa) is intoxication. The conclusion is 'Wine is forbidden.' Persuasive thinking requires stating both premise and cause.
This is a basic qiyas argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Audience Acceptability in Rhetorical Arguments
Author gives the rule for selecting rhetorical opinions based on audience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a rhetorical opinion is appropriate for a given audience based on the author's criteria of acceptability.

Core Idea
Rhetorical arguments succeed only if premises are acceptable to the audience or authorities.

The passage concludes with an important practical principle: rhetorical opinions (al-ara' al-khitabiyya) must be 'chosen and acceptable' to each individual addressed or to the imams. This lesson applies that principle to real da'wa and debate scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Find the criterion
Locate the sentence: 'And it is proper that the rhetorical opinions be chosen opinions, acceptable to each individual whom one addresses, or to the authorities.'
This is the key principle for audience adaptation.
2
Understand 'chosen and acceptable'
'Chosen' means the arguer selects premises strategically. 'Acceptable' means the audience already believes them or is willing to accept them. This ensures persuasion.
3
Identify the two categories of audience
The author mentions two: specific individuals (each person addressed) and authorities (al-a'imma). For a layperson, use common sense premises; for a scholar, use authoritative texts.
4
Apply to a scenario
Suppose you are debating a Mu'tazili who denies that God can be seen in the afterlife. A suitable acceptable premise might be a Qur'anic verse they accept, rather than a philosophical argument they reject.
This is a standard practice in munazara.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Thinking is described as an opinion that is close to the conclusion—i.e., a premise that leads directly to the desired conclusion. The author gives an example: 'Knowledge of events through wisdom is superfluous'—this is an opinion (premise). Its result (conclusion) is that those who do so store what they do not benefit from. But a conclusion taken alone is not persuasive; it must be attached to its cause (العلة), i.e., the reasoning. Thus, for a rhetorical argument to be persuasive, the cause must be stated. However, sometimes if the conclusion is obvious, it does not need the cause. The author emphasizes that rhetorical opinions should be chosen and acceptable to the specific audience or to the authorities (imams). This reflects the principle of tailoring arguments in Islamic da'wa and debate: one must use premises that the audience already accepts. For example, when debating a Mu'tazili, one uses premises they accept; with a layperson, commonsense examples.

Key Terms
الرأي المحمود
التفكير الإقناعي
العلة

D) They must be results of thinking

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical arguments must use premises that the audience already accepts or that are backed by recognized authorities.
