Deriving Arguments from Universal Judgments
Advice on deriving arguments from universal judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive a rhetorical enthymeme from a limited universal judgment and tailor it to a specific audience.

Core Idea
Use few universal judgments, not infinite particulars, to craft persuasive arguments.

The author shows how to move from general principles to specific arguments. Once you master the rules of rhetoric, you can derive arguments (hujaj) and enthymemes (dama'ir) efficiently. The key is to rely on limited universal judgments (al-ahkam al-kulliyya al-munhasira) that cover many cases, rather than trying to address every particular detail. Then specialize these judgments to make them more beneficial and appealing to the listener's mind.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal judgment
Find a general principle from the three genera of rhetoric (e.g., all divine actions are praiseworthy) that can serve as a starting point.
2
Specialize the judgment
Narrow the universal statement to a specific case relevant to your argument, e.g., from 'divine power is good' to 'this leader's justice reflects divine power'.
3
Consider the listener's condition
Assess the listener's background, needs, and receptivity. Adjust the statement (qawl kulli) to resonate with them, ensuring appropriateness.
4
Formulate the enthymeme
Combine the specialized judgment with relevant facts to produce a concise rhetorical syllogism, leaving some premises implicit for effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان هذا محصلا عندك أمكنك أن تستنبط منه الحجج والضمائر ولا يجب عليك أن تضبط أمورًا غير متناهية بحس شخص شخص في أمر أمر جزئي بل أن تضيف الأحكام الكلية المنحصرة الموجودة المتعلقة بالأجناس الثلاثة للخطابة، وتجتهد أن تخصصها ما قدرت. فإن الأحكام التي هي أخص أشد نفعا، وأقرب إلى الألباب. وأما الأحكام فمنها ما يكون مدحا من غير شك مثل قولنا إن فلاناً إلهي، وإن فيه قوة إلهية، ومنها ما يكون ذمّا كذلك كقولنا إن لفلان نفسًا شيطانية أو نفسا سبعية. ومنها ما يكون صالحًا لهذا وذلك؛ وإن كانا من الأضداد كقولك اعرف ذاتك وانظر إلى نفسك أو إلى جسدك أو إلى نسبك مثلا؛ وينبغي أن يكون المتكلم بصيرًا بحال السامع وحاجته بالقول الكلي حتى يكون الكلام مناسبًا.
And if this is attained with you, you can derive from it arguments and assumptions (or enthymemes). And you should not confine yourself to infinite matters by the sense of each individual in each particular matter, but rather you should attach the limited comprehensive judgments related to the three genera of rhetoric, and strive to specialize them as much as you can. For the judgments that are more specific are more beneficial and closer to minds. As for judgments, some are undoubtedly praise, like our saying: 'So-and-so is divine, and in him is divine power.' And some are undoubtedly blame, like our saying: 'So-and-so has a satanic soul or a bestial soul.' And some are suitable for both this and that, even if they are opposites, like your saying: 'Know yourself, and look to your self, or to your body, or to your lineage,' for example. And it is proper for the speaker to be aware of the condition of the listener and his need for the universal statement, so that the speech is appropriate.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This final passage provides practical advice on deriving rhetorical arguments (حجج) and enthymemes (ضمائر). The author says that once one has grasped the rules and types discussed, one can derive arguments. He warns against trying to cover every particular case individually (infinite), but instead one should apply the limited universal judgments (الأحكام الكلية المنحصرة) that belong to the three genera of rhetoric (persuasive, judicial, demonstrative). Then one should specialize them as much as possible, because specific judgments are more beneficial and closer to the intellects (أقرب إلى الألباب). He then gives examples of judgments: praise (مدح) such as saying someone has divine power (قوة إلهية) – this is eulogy; blame (ذم) such as saying someone has a satanic soul (نفس شيطانية) or bestial soul (نفس سبعية). Some judgments can be used for both praise and blame depending on context, like the command 'Know yourself' (اعرف ذاتك). This Socratic maxim can be used to praise self-awareness or blame ignorance. Finally, he stresses that the speaker must be perceptive of the listener's condition and need for a universal statement (قول كلي), so that the speech is appropriate (مناسب). This highlights the rhetorical principle of audience adaptation, essential in Islamic da'wa, where a speaker must consider the listener's background and understanding.

Key Terms

الأحكام الكلية المنحصرة
الأجناس الثلاثة
تخصص

Categorizing Judgments: Praise, Blame, and Neutral
Examples of praise, blame, and context-sensitive judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a rhetorical judgment as praise, blame, or context-sensitive, and explain how a single statement can serve both purposes.

Core Idea
Some judgments are clearly praise or blame; others shift meaning with context.

The author illustrates three types of judgments: clear praise (e.g., calling someone 'divine'), clear blame (e.g., 'satanic soul'), and statements that can imply either depending on context (e.g., 'Know yourself'). The speaker must understand the listener's situation to use these effectively. This categorization is essential for crafting appropriate arguments in Islamic rhetoric and da'wa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judgment's inherent value
Determine if the judgment is universally positive (praise) or negative (blame) in its normal usage. For example, 'divine power' is always praiseworthy in a religious context.
2
Check for contextual flexibility
Some statements like 'Know yourself' can be praise when meaning self-awareness, or blame when implying ignorance. Note if the judgment is inherently ambiguous.
3
Consider the speaker's intent and audience
The speaker's goal and the listener's condition determine how the judgment is received. A pharaoh might use 'Know yourself' to praise arrogance, while a prophet uses it to urge humility.
4
Apply in argument construction
Use the appropriate type of judgment to support your case. For praising a scholar, use clear praise; for criticizing hypocrisy, use clear blame; for nuanced advice, use a neutral statement that context will clarify.

Common Assents: Distinguishing Opinion from Thinking
Author defines common assents and distinguishes example from thinking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ra'y (opinion) and tafkiri (thinking) in rhetorical reasoning, and explain how an opinion becomes persuasive thinking when coupled with a cause.

Core Idea
Common assents are the raw material of rhetoric; opinion is universal, thinking is opinion plus cause.

In this passage, the author introduces the two types of common assents used in rhetoric: example and thinking. He explains that an opinion (ra'y) is a universal judgment about benefit or harm, but it does not produce assent by itself. It becomes persuasive thinking (tafkiri) only when coupled with a cause ('illa). Understanding this distinction is key to constructing effective rhetorical arguments in Islamic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of common assents
The author states that common assents are of two types: example (al-mithal) and thinking (al-tafkiri). Note that this lesson focuses on thinking; example is mentioned but not elaborated.
2
Distinguish opinion (ra'y) from thinking (tafkiri)
Find how the author defines each. Opinion is a universal judgment in practical matters. Thinking is an opinion close to the conclusion that becomes persuasive when coupled with a cause.
Ra'y is not accepted by itself; it requires a cause to produce assent.
3
Understand the role of the cause ('illa)
The passage says thinking becomes persuasive thinking 'if it is coupled with the cause'. Identify what the cause does: it makes the opinion acceptable by providing a reason.
Without the cause, the opinion is not beneficial.
4
Apply the concept to a rhetorical example
The author gives an example: 'Knowledge of events through wisdom is superfluous'. Analyse why this is an opinion, and what would make it persuasive thinking (e.g., adding a cause like 'because it leads to no benefit').
5
Extract the practical implication for constructing arguments
The author advises that opinions should be selected based on the addressee, and then one can derive arguments and enthymemes from them. How does understanding common assents help in crafting persuasive speech?
More specific judgments are more beneficial and closer to minds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أنواع التعظيم والتحقير فقد يكتفي فيه بما ذكر في المشوريات؛ وخصوصا بحسب أمر أمر من الأمور الجزئية فلنفصل الأمر في التصديقات المشتركة وهي جنسان المثال والتفكير. وأما الرأي المحمود فهو داخل في مواد التفكير؛ والتفكير أولى أن يوقع التصديق. وأما الرأي فإنه قضية كلية لا جزئية وهي في أمور علمية، ومن جهة ما يؤثر أو يجتنب والتفكير رأي قريب من النتيجة؛ ونتائج الآراء إذا أخذت بانفرادها هي أيضًا آراء كما أن مقدماتها آراء لكنها إنما تكون تفكيرًا إقناعيًا إذا قرنت به العلة كما في قولنا إن معرفة الأحداث بالحكمة فضول فهو رأي، ونتيجة رأي هو أنهم حينئذ يكونون مدخرين ما لا ينتفعون به. والرأي الذي هو نتيجة إذا أخذت وحده لم ينتفع به؛ إذ ليس مقبولا بنفسه؛ إذ القبول بعد قبول مقدمة هي علة والرأي قد يحتاج إلى أن يقرن بكلام آخر، وقد لا يحتاج لظهوره عند المخاطب مثلا، وينبغي أن تكون الآراء الخطابية آراء مختارة ومقبولة عند إنسان إنسان ممن يخاطبه أو عند الأئمة . وإذا كان هذا محصلا عندك أمكنك أن تستنبط منه الحجج والضمائر ولا يجب عليك أن تضبط أمورًا غير متناهية بحس شخص شخص في أمر أمر جزئي بل أن تضيف الأحكام الكلية المنحصرة الموجودة المتعلقة بالأجناس الثلاثة للخطابة، وتجتهد أن تخصصها ما قدرت. فإن الأحكام التي هي أخص أشد نفعا، وأقرب إلى الألباب.
As for the types of glorification and belittlement, one may be satisfied with what was mentioned in the deliberations; especially according to each particular matter. So let us detail the matter concerning the common assents, which are two kinds: the example and the thinking. As for the praiseworthy opinion, it falls within the materials of thinking; and thinking is more apt to produce assent. As for the opinion, it is a universal judgment, not a particular one, and it concerns practical matters, from the perspective of what is beneficial or to be avoided. And thinking is an opinion close to the conclusion; and the conclusions of opinions, when taken by themselves, are also opinions just as their premises are opinions, but they become persuasive thinking only when the cause is coupled with them, as in our saying: 'Knowledge of events through wisdom is superfluous'; so it is an opinion, and the result of the opinion is that they then are storing what they do not benefit from. The opinion which is a conclusion, when taken alone, is not beneficial; for it is not acceptable by itself; since acceptance comes after acceptance of a premise which is a cause. And an opinion may need to be coupled with another statement, or may not need that due to its clarity to the addressee, for example. It is necessary that rhetorical opinions be selected and acceptable to each person one addresses or to the authorities. If this is established for you, you can derive from it the arguments and enthymemes, and you are not required to grasp infinite matters by the sense of a person in each particular matter; rather, you should add the limited universal judgments that exist pertaining to the three genera of rhetoric, and strive to specify them as much as you can. For judgments that are more specific are more beneficial and closer to minds.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage defines the common assents (al-tasdiqat al-mushtaraka) used in rhetoric, which are of two types: example (al-mithal) and thinking (al-tafkiri). The author explains that a praiseworthy opinion (al-ra'y al-mahmud) falls within the materials of thinking, and thinking is more likely to produce assent. An opinion (ra'y) is a universal judgment concerning practical matters, whether beneficial or harmful. Thinking (tafkiri) is an opinion close to the conclusion; when the conclusion of an opinion is taken alone, it is still an opinion, but it becomes persuasive thinking only when coupled with a cause ('illa). The author gives an example: 'Knowledge of events through wisdom is superfluous' — this is an opinion, and its result is that people are storing what they do not benefit from. An opinion taken alone is not beneficial because it is not accepted by itself; acceptance requires a premise that is a cause. Some opinions need to be coupled with other statements, while others do not due to their clarity to the audience. The author then advises that rhetorical opinions should be selected and acceptable to the specific addressee or to authorities. Once this is understood, one can derive arguments (hujaj) and enthymemes (damair) from them without having to grasp infinite particular matters. Instead, one should add the universal judgments that are limited and pertain to the three genera of rhetoric (presumably praise, blame, and neutral), and strive to specify them, since more specific judgments are more beneficial and closer to the minds of the audience.

Key Terms
التصديقات المشتركة (al-tasdiqat al-mushtaraka): The common assents: two types of rhetorical propositions, namely example (mithal) and thinking (tafkiri).
المثال (al-mithal): Example: a type of common assent that produces assent through an instance or analogy. [Note: In other contexts, mithal can refer to a syllogism by example (qiyas tamthili) in logic.]
التفكير (al-tafkiri): Thinking: a type of common assent that is an opinion close to the conclusion, becoming persuasive when coupled with a cause. [Note: This term is often used for deliberation; here it is a specific rhetorical category.]
الرأي (al-ra'y): Opinion: a universal judgment in practical matters concerning benefit or harm, not accepted by itself but requiring a cause. [Note: In dialectic, ra'y may refer to generally accepted opinion; here it is a constituent of rhetorical thinking.]
العلة (al-illa): Cause: the justification that, when coupled with an opinion, makes it persuasive thinking. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, 'illa is the effective cause for a legal ruling; here it is a logical cause for persuasion.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Ra'y is a universal judgment and tafkiri is an opinion close to the conclusion that becomes persuasive when coupled with a cause.
C) Ra'y is based on certainty, tafkiri on probability.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
An opinion (ra'y) alone does not persuade; it becomes persuasive thinking (tafkiri) only when coupled with a cause ('illa) that gives it acceptability.

Rhetorical Judgments and the Difference from Dialectic
Author classifies rhetorical judgments and contrasts rhetoric with dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify rhetorical judgments into praise, blame, and neutral, and distinguish rhetorical premises from dialectical premises.

Core Idea
Rhetoric uses persuasive premises that stir opinion, not remote facts like dialectic.

In this passage, the author categorizes the types of judgments used in rhetoric and then contrasts rhetoric with dialectic. Understanding these categories helps you recognize how rhetoric functions in Islamic persuasion, from Friday sermons to political speeches. The key is the nature of the premises: rhetorical premises are close to the listener's mind and produce opinion or doubt, while dialectical premises are often well-known truths that may be remote from the desired conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of judgments in rhetoric
Look for the three categories: praise (madh), blame (dhamm), and neutral (both). The author gives examples: calling someone 'divine' is praise; 'satanic soul' is blame; 'know yourself' can be used for both.
2
Understand the requirement for the speaker
The speaker must be perceptive about the listener's state and need, so the speech suits the desired goal in rhetoric.
3
Compare rhetoric and dialectic
Note that the desired goal differs because the premises differ. Dialectical premises can be remote from the goal, but rhetorical premises cannot be extremely remote.
4
Recognize the nature of rhetorical premises
Rhetorical premises are not necessarily well-known truths (mashhurat) nor absent; they are premises that, when mentioned, produce an opinion or doubt, even if not present in the mind initially.
Example: 'How bad is greed!' is a rhetorical premise that fits this description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأحكام فمنها ما يكون مدحا من غير شك مثل قولنا إن فلاناً إلهي، وإن فيه قوة إلهية، ومنها ما يكون ذمّا كذلك كقولنا إن لفلان نفسًا شيطانية أو نفسا سبعية. ومنها ما يكون صالحًا لهذا وذلك ؛ وإن كانا من الأضداد كقولك اعرف ذاتك وانظر إلى نفسك أو إلى جسدك أو إلى نسبك مثلا؛ وينبغي أن يكون المتكلم بصيرًا بحال السامع وحاجته بالقول الكلي حتى يكون الكلام مناسبًا للمطلوب في الخطابة والخطابة كما تكون للمدح والذم فكذلك لدفع الذم؛ والمطلوب في الخطابة غير المطلوب في الجدل لما كانت المقدّمات الخطابية غير المقدّمات الجدلية. ولا يبعد أن تكون المقدّمات الجدلية هي البعيدة عن المطلوب بخلاف الخطابة فإنه لا يصح أن تستعمل فيها المقدمات البعيدة حدا؛ ولا الشرط فيها أن تستعمل المشهورات الحقيقية فقط، ولا المقدمات البعيدة عن ظنون الجمهور بل إنها تستعمل فيها ما لا يكون حاضرا في الأذهان بالفعل مثل كون الشمس مشرقة، ولا غائبًا عنها كذلك وإلا لكانت الأذهان متقاعدة عن الحكم فيها بوجه بل هي التي عند ما تذكر يتقدم فيها ظن أو يقدح منها ذلك إذا ذكرت مفردة أو مع قرينة كما مر . وهذا مثل قولهم بئس الشيء الطمع ; وما يكون مناسبًا بطباع العامة فهو مثل هذا الكلام
As for the judgments, some of them are without doubt praise, such as our saying: 'So-and-so is divine, and there is a divine power in him.' And some are blame likewise, such as our saying: 'So-and-so has a satanic soul or a bestial soul.' And some are suitable for both, even if they are opposites, like your saying: 'Know your self, and look to your soul, or your body, or your lineage,' for example. And it is necessary that the speaker be perceptive about the state of the listener and his need by the universal statement so that the speech is suitable for the desired goal in rhetoric. And rhetoric is for praise and blame, and likewise for repelling blame. The desired goal in rhetoric is different from the desired goal in dialectic because rhetorical premises are different from dialectical premises. It is not unlikely that dialectical premises are remote from the desired goal, unlike rhetoric, for it is not valid to use extremely remote premises in it. Nor is the condition in it that only actual well-known truths be used, nor premises remote from the opinions of the public; rather, it uses what is not actually present in minds, such as that the sun is shining, nor is it absent from them likewise; otherwise minds would be disinclined to judge it at all. Rather, it is such that when remembered, an opinion emerges or doubt arises from them if mentioned alone or with a contextual clue, as has passed. This is like their saying: 'How bad is greed!' And what is suitable to the nature of the common people is such speech.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of judgments (ahkam) used in rhetoric into three categories: those that are clearly praise (madh), such as calling someone divine; those that are clearly blame (dhamm), such as having a satanic soul; and those that can be used for both, like the statement 'Know yourself' which can be praise or blame depending on context. The author then emphasizes that the speaker must be aware of the listener's state and need so that the speech is appropriate for the rhetorical goal. Rhetoric is used both for praise and for repelling blame. The goal in rhetoric differs from that in dialectic because rhetorical premises differ from dialectical premises. Dialectical premises may be remote from the desired conclusion, but in rhetoric one cannot use extremely remote premises. Moreover, rhetoric does not require only actual well-known truths (mashhurat haqiqiyya) nor premises that are far from public opinion. Instead, it uses premises that are not immediately present in the mind but, when mentioned, produce an opinion or doubt, whether mentioned alone or with a contextual clue. An example is the commonly used saying 'How bad is greed!' Such speech is suitable for the common people.

Key Terms
المدح
الذم
الخطابة
الجدل

C) Premises that are not present in the mind but when mentioned produce an opinion or doubt.
D) Only premises that are remote and require demonstration.

Key Takeaway
Rhetoric uses premises that are not currently in the mind but, when mentioned, generate opinion or doubt — unlike dialectic which often uses well-known truths.

The Three Modes of Rhetorical Speech: Fallacy, Enticement, Confession
Author categorises rhetorical speech into three modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three rhetorical modes (mughalata, istidraj, i'tiraf) and identify which mode a given rhetorical argument belongs to.

Core Idea
Rhetorical speech exploits three modes: fallacy, enticement, and confession.

In this passage, the author introduces the three fundamental modes of rhetorical speech within the logical tradition. Understanding these modes allows you to classify rhetorical arguments and assess their persuasive strategies. The commentary explains each mode with the author's own distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modes
Find the three terms: mughalata (fallacy), istidraj (enticement), and i'tiraf (confession). Note that each is introduced with 'قد يقع بوجه ...'.
These are not actual logical arguments but rhetorical devices.
2
Understand the distinguishing features
Fallacy uses deceptive reasoning; enticement requires a question to be informative; confession uses distorted enthymemes passed off as valid.
Enticement is the only one that depends on an audience question.
3
Apply to an Islamic argument
Consider a mutakallim who says 'Either the world is eternal or created; it is not eternal, so it is created.' This is a valid syllogism, not rhetorical. Think of an example where the speaker uses emotional appeal instead of logical proof: that would be mughalata or istidraj.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الكلام في الخطابة قد يقع بوجه المغالطة، وقد يقع بوجه الاستدراج وهو الذي يفتقر في الإفادة إلى سؤال، وقد يقع بوجه الاعتراف كما مر من قبل أن استعمال الضمائر المحرفة التي ليست حقيقية قد يكون خطابيا: فمنها ما تحريفه بسبب اللفظ كالذي يكون فيه لفظ مشترك أو ما يجري مجراه، ومنها ما تحريفه بسبب الكل وهو أن لا يكون القول يلزم عنه الأمر بحال لكن القائل يتكلف وينتقل عن القول إلى النتيجة فيروجها. فمن ذلك ما يكون باشتراك الاسم الصرف كمن يمدح الكلب فيقول ألا ترى أن الكلب الذي في السماء سائر الكواكب نورا فمن ذلك ما يكون بسبب التركيب والتفصيل كمن يقول فلان يعرف الحروف والهجاء فيعرف إذا الشعر، وكقولهم كيف يكون عن شرير خير. وقد يقال هذا على جهة التوبيخ ، ويقال على جهة التثبيت؛ ومن ذلك أن يترك الأمر وينتقل إلى غيره مثل المفكّر أنه فعل شيئًا اتهم به إذا لم يكن عنده حجة يبين بها أنه لم يفعله؛ فإنه يأخذ في تقبيح من يفعل ذلك الفعل
Then speech in rhetoric may occur by way of fallacy (mughalata), and may occur by way of enticement (istidraj), which is that which needs a question to be informative, and may occur by way of confession (i'tiraf) , as has passed, because the use of distorted enthymemes that are not genuine may be rhetorical: among them are those whose distortion is due to wording, such as where there is a shared term or what runs its course; and among them are those whose distortion is due to the whole, which is that the statement does not entail the matter at all, but the speaker exerts himself and shifts from the statement to the conclusion and passes it off. Some of that is due to pure homonymy, like one who praises a dog by saying: 'Do you not see that the dog in the sky (Canis Major) surpasses the other planets in light?' And some of that is due to composition and analysis, like one who says: 'So-and-so knows letters and spelling, so he knows poetry'; and like their saying: 'How can good come from an evil person?' And this may be said by way of rebuke, and may be said by way of confirmation; and among that is to leave the matter and shift to another, like the suspect who, when accused of something, if he does not have a proof to show he did not do it, he starts to condemn the one who does such an act.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage discusses the three modes in which rhetorical speech occurs: (1) by way of fallacy (mughalata), (2) by way of enticement (istidraj), which requires a question to be beneficial, and (3) by way of confession (i'tiraf). The focus then turns to the use of distorted enthymemes (damair muharrafa) that are not genuine but are used rhetorically. These distortions are of two types: (a) distortion due to wording (bi-sabab al-lafz), such as when an ambiguous or homonymous term is used; and (b) distortion due to the whole (bi-sabab al-kull), meaning the statement does not entail the conclusion at all, but the speaker forces it and shifts from the statement to the conclusion to pass it off. Examples of homonymy include praising a mere dog by referring to the constellation Canis Major (the dog in the sky) which is bright. An example of distortion due to composition is saying that someone knows letters and spelling, so he knows poetry; this is invalid because knowing letters does not entail knowing poetry. Another example is the saying 'How can good come from an evil person?' which may be used as a rebuke or as a confirmation. Finally, the author mentions the tactic of shifting from the original issue to something else, such as when an accused person, lacking evidence, instead starts condemning the act of which he is accused.

Key Terms
المغالطة
الاستدراج
الاعتراف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which mode of rhetorical speech requires a question from the audience to be informative?
A) Fallacy (mughalata)
B) Enticement (istidraj)
C) Confession (i'tiraf)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical speech is classified into three modes: fallacy, enticement (which needs a question), and confession (which uses distorted enthymemes).

Two Types of Distortion in Rhetorical Enthymemes
Author details two types of distortion in rhetorical enthymemes with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify distortion by wording (tahrif bi al-lafz) and distortion by the whole (tahrif bi al-kull) in rhetorical arguments, and explain why the given examples are fallacious.

Core Idea
Distortion in rhetoric arises either from ambiguous wording or an invalid leap to the conclusion.

After introducing the three modes, the author focuses on the use of distorted enthymemes. He identifies two types of distortion: one due to ambiguous wording (e.g., homonymy) and one due to a missing logical connection (the whole). The commentary unpacks each type with clear examples from the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of distortion
In the Arabic, find 'فمنها ما تحريفه بسبب اللفظ' and 'ومنها ما تحريفه بسبب الكل'. Note that 'بسبب اللفظ' deals with ambiguous terms, and 'بسبب الكل' deals with the statement not entailing the conclusion.
The phrase 'بسبب الكل' is unique; it means the whole argument is invalid.
2
Analyse the example of homonymy
The author gives the example of praising a mere dog by calling it 'the dog in the sky' (Canis Major). This is fallacious because the word 'dog' is shared between the animal and the constellation. The argument falsely transfers the brightness of the constellation to the animal.
This is a classic fallacy of equivocation.
3
Analyse the example of invalid composition
The example: 'So-and-so knows letters and spelling, so he knows poetry.' The author says this is due to composition (tarkib). The fallacy is that knowing letters does not entail knowing poetry; the composition is invalid.
This is an example of distortion by the whole, because the statement does not entail the conclusion.
4
Recognise the shift tactic
The author mentions a suspect who, lacking evidence, shifts to condemning the act itself. This is a diversionary tactic, also a form of distortion by the whole.
This is common in polemics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Some of that is due to pure homonymy, like one who praises a dog by saying: 'Do you not see that the dog in the sky (Canis Major) surpasses the other planets in light?' And some of that is due to composition and analysis, like one who says: 'So-and-so knows letters and spelling, so he knows poetry'; and like their saying: 'How can good come from an evil person?' And this may be said by way of rebuke, and may be said by way of confirmation; and among that is to leave the matter and shift to another, like the suspect who, when accused of something, if he does not have a proof to show he did not do it, he starts to condemn the one who does such an act.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage discusses the three modes in which rhetorical speech occurs: (1) by way of fallacy (mughalata), (2) by way of enticement (istidraj), which requires a question to be beneficial, and (3) by way of confession (i'tiraf). The focus then turns to the use of distorted enthymemes (damair muharrafa) that are not genuine but are used rhetorically. The Definition of Poetry: Imaginative Speech and Meter
Author defines poetry as imaginative speech with meter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given composition qualifies as poetry according to the Islamic logical tradition by checking for the presence of both imaginative speech (قول مخيل) and meter (وزن).

Core Idea
Poetry requires both imaginative speech and meter – one without the other is not poetry.

In this lesson, we examine the precise definition of poetry as presented in the Islamic logical tradition. The author distinguishes poetry from mere metrical prose or imaginative speech alone, grounding it in a combination of two essential components. Understanding this definition is crucial for correctly classifying texts within the five arts of logic and for appreciating how poetry functions as a tool for affecting the soul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the presence of meter
Check whether the composition has a consistent rhythmic pattern (وزن). The author notes that meter alone without imaginative speech is not poetry (e.g., dance rhythms).
2
Identify the presence of imaginative speech
Determine whether the speech creates a mental image or emotional response (قول مخيل). Prose with imaginative content but no meter is not poetry.
3
Check for the combination
The author states: 'وإنما يجوز الشعر بأن يجتمع فيه القول المخيل والوزن' — poetry is valid only when both are combined. If either is missing, it is not poetry.
4
Apply the definition to examples
Contrast Greek poetry (which aims at imitating actions) with Arab poetry (which aims at affecting the soul). Both forms still require the combination of imaginative speech and meter.
This highlights that the definition is universal, irrespective of cultural purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في النفس تأثيرا لا يرتاب به. ولكل غرض لحن يليق به بحسب جزالة أو لينة أو بتوسطه. وبذلك التأثير تصير النفس محاكية في نفسها لحزن أو غضب أو غير ذلك، وبالكلام نفسه إذا كان مخيلا محاكيًا، وبالوزن؛ فإن من الأوزان ما يطلس ومنها ما يوقر. وربَّما اجتمعت هذه كلها، وربَّما انفرد الوزن والكلام المخيل؛ فإن هذه الأشياء قد يفترق بعضها من بعض. وذلك أن اللحن المركب من نغم متفقة ومن إيقاع قد يوجد في المعارف والمزاهير واللحن المفرد الذي لا إيقاع فيه قد يوجد في المزامير المرسلة التي لا يوقع عليها الأصابع إذا استويت منسابة والإيقاع الذي لا لحن فيه قد يوجد في الرقص؛ ولذلك فإن الرقص مشكل جدًّا بمقارنته اللحن إياه حتى يؤثر في النفس قد تكون أقاويل منثورة مخيلة، وقد تكون أوزان غير مخيلة لأنها ساذجة لا قول. وإنما يجوز الشعر بأن يجتمع فيه القول المخيل والوزن فإن الأقاويل الموزونة التي عملها عدة من الفلاسة ومنهم سقراط قد وزنــت إما بوزن المؤلف من أربعة عشر رجلا، وإما بوزن المؤلف من ستة عشر رجلا وغير ذلك. فإن كان مؤلّف من أربعة وعشرين رجلًا وهي المقاطع. وبالجملة فالأشعار اليونانية إنما كان يقصد فيها في أكثر الأمر محاكاة الأفعال والأحوال بخلاف العرب؛ فإن شعرهم إما / لأن يؤثر في النفس كما مر، وإما للتعجب [٣٦٤] فقط. فاليونانيون كانوا يقصدون أن يحبّوا بالقول على فعل أو يردعوا بالقول عن فعل. وكانوا يفعلون ذلك تارة على سبيل الحكاية، وتارة على سبيل الشعر؛ فلذلك كانت المحاكاة عندهم مقصورة على الأفاعيل والأقوال. ثم المحاكاة مشتملة على هذه الثلاثة: [1] تشبيه، [ب] وتركيب، [ت] واستعارة؛ والأغراض ثلاثة كذلك [1] تحسين [ب] وتقبيح [ت] ومطابقة. والمطابقة من جملة ما يمكن أن يمال به إلى حسن، وأن يمال به إلى قبح كمن شبَّه شرف النفس الغضبية بوثب الأسد فإنه مطابقة يمكن أن يمال إلى الجانبين؛ فإنه يقال بوثب الأسد الضاري، أو بوثب الأسد المقدام. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن السبب المولد للشعر شيئان: [1] أحدهما الالتذاذ بالمحاكاة واستعمالها منذ الصبى؛ وللمحاكاة التي في الناس فائدة وذلك في الإشارة التي يحاكي بها المعاني فتقوم مقام التعليم، ويقع موقع سائر الأمور المتقدمة على التعليم أو حتى أن الإشارة إذا قربت بالعبارة أوقعت المعنى في النفس إيقاعا جليًّا. [ب] والثاني في حبّ الناس للتأليف المتفق والألحان طبعا. ثم قد وجدت الأوزان مناسبة للألحان فمالت إليها الأنفس وأوجد بها. فمن هذين الشيئين تولدت الشعرية وجعلت تنحو يسيرًا يسيرًا تابعة للطباع، وأكثر تولدها عن المطبوعين الذين يرتحلون الشعر طبعا انبعثت الشعرية منهم بحسب غريزة كل واحد منهم وقريحته وبحسب خلقه وعادته. فمن كان منهم أعفٌ مال إلى المحاكاة بالأفعال الجميلة أو بما يشاكله، ومن كان منهم أخس مال إلى الهجاء أو نحو ذلك من الرزائل. والمتقدمون لما تعرضوا للشعر على أوزان مختلفة كما سبق ذكرها من قبل فقد ينشأ من عمل ساطوري وهي الرباعيات من نوع وزن تذكر فيها المشهورات، والأمثال أوزان الرباعيات المشهورة بين الأمة وهي الأوزان المأخوذة من نوعي المجز أعني الأمرت والأحزم اثنا عشر من هذا النوع واثنا عشر من ذلك؛ والجملة أربعة وعشرون ليس إلا؛ وقد بينت المجموع في الرسالة المنسوبة إلى هذا الفن، ورسمتُ الكل في دائرة على سبيل الاختصار. وقد قيل في تسمية هذا النوع ساطوري إن الطبيعة صادقة ملائم الرقص المسمى ساطوريقا؛ فلهذا يسمى بهذا الاسم.
It has an effect in the soul that is not doubted. For every purpose there is a tone suitable to it according to grandeur or softness or moderation. And by that effect the soul becomes imitating in itself sadness or anger or other than that, and by the speech itself if it is imaginative and imitative, and by meter; for among meters are those that make cheerful and those that make solemn. And perhaps all these are combined, and perhaps the meter and the imaginative speech are alone; for these things may separate from one another. That is because the compound melody of consistent notes and rhythm may be found in melodies and songs, and the simple melody without rhythm may be found in the untrammeled pipes on which fingers are not placed when they are flowing evenly, and rhythm without melody may be found in dance; therefore dance is very problematic by comparing the melody to it until it affects the soul. There may be prose speeches that are imaginative, and there may be meters that are not imaginative because they are bare without speech. And poetry is valid only when imaginative speech and meter are combined together. For the metrical speeches composed by several philosophers including Socrates were weighed either by a meter composed of fourteen feet, or by a meter composed of sixteen feet, etc. If it is composed of twenty-four feet, those are the verses. In general, Greek poetry intended for the most part imitation of actions and states, contrary to the Arabs; for their poetry is either to affect the soul as mentioned, or only for wonder [364]. So the Greeks intended to incite by speech to an action or to deter by speech from an action. And they did that sometimes by way of narrative, and sometimes by way of poetry; therefore imitation with them was confined to actions and sayings. Then imitation includes these three: [1] analogy, [b] composition, [t] and metaphor; and the purposes are likewise three: [1] beautification, [b] debasement, [t] and matching. And matching is among that which can be inclined to good or can be inclined to evil, like one who likens the honor of the irascible soul to the leap of the lion, for it is a match that can be inclined to both sides; for it is said 'the leap of the predatory lion' or 'the leap of the brave lion'. If you know this, we say: The generative cause of poetry is two things: [1] One is delight in imitation and its use since childhood; and imitation has a benefit in people, namely in the indication by which meanings are imitated so that it takes the place of instruction, and it occurs in the place of other things prior to instruction or so that indication, when brought close by expression, places the meaning in the soul with a clear impact. [b] The second is people's natural love for harmonious composition and melodies. Then meters were found suitable for melodies, so the souls inclined to them and were affected by them. From these two things poetry was generated and it began to proceed little by little following natural dispositions. Most of its generation comes from naturally gifted people who spontaneously emit poetry; poetry emanated from them according to the instinct and talent of each one, and according to his nature and habit. He among them who was pure inclined to imitation of good actions or what resembles them, and he who was base inclined to satire or similar vices. The ancients, when they undertook poetry on different meters as previously mentioned, there might arise from the work of satory, which are quatrains of a type of meter in which commonplaces and proverbs are mentioned. The meters of the famous quatrains among the people are the meters taken from two types of the partial meters, namely al-amart and al-ahzam: twelve from this type and twelve from that; the total is twenty-four only. And I have explained the sum in the epistle attributed to this art, and I have drawn everything in a circle by way of abbreviation. And it was said regarding the naming of this type 'satory' that nature is truthful in agreeing with the dance called satorika; therefore it is named by this name.
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory treatment of poetry within the logical tradition. The author begins by discussing the psychological effects of melody, tone, and meter, noting that different purposes require different tonal qualities. These elements combine in the soul to produce imitation of emotions. Poetry proper is defined as the combination of imaginative speech (قول مخيل) and meter (وزن). The author contrasts this with mere melodic or rhythmic elements that may exist separately: compound melody in songs, simple melody in wind instruments, rhythm in dance, and prose with imaginative speech but no meter. He then emphasizes that poetry only occurs when both imaginative speech and meter are combined. As an example, he mentions the metrical works of philosophers like Socrates, which used feet of 14, 16, or 24 units. The author contrasts Greek poetry, which primarily aims at imitating actions and states to incite or deter, with Arab poetry, which aims at affecting the soul or producing wonder. He then details that imitation (محاكاة) comprises three components: analogy (تشبيه), composition (تركيب), and metaphor (استعارة), serving three purposes: beautification (تحسين), debasement (تقبيح), and matching (مطابقة). He illustrates matching with the example of likening the honor of the irascible soul to a lion's leap, which can be described positively or negatively. Finally, the author identifies two generative causes of poetry: (1) innate delight in imitation from childhood, which serves an educational function through indicative imitation, and (2) natural love for harmonious composition and melody, with meters aligning to melodies. From these two, poetry arises gradually, shaped by natural disposition: the pure incline toward noble imitation, the base toward satire. The passage concludes with a discussion of poetic meters, specifically the satory (رباعيات) meters, which are twenty-four in number, as explained in the author's own epistle.

The Two Generative Causes of Poetry
Author explains the two generative causes of poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two generative causes of poetry (delight in imitation and love for harmonious composition) and explain how they work together to produce poetic expression.

Core Idea
Poetry arises from innate delight in imitation and natural love for harmonious composition.

After defining poetry's essential components, the author turns to its psychological and natural origins. He identifies two deep-seated human tendencies that generate poetry: the delight in imitation present since childhood, and the love for harmonious composition and melody. Understanding these causes helps explain why poetry is a universal human phenomenon and how it reflects the poet's natural disposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first cause: delight in imitation
The author states that the first generative cause is 'الالتذاذ بالمحاكاة' – delight in imitation. This is a natural human tendency from childhood, serving an educational function by using imitation to convey meanings.
Imitation here refers to representing actions, emotions, or ideas through speech.
2
Identify the second cause: love for harmonious composition
The second cause is 'حب التأليف المتفق والألحان' – love for harmonious composition and melodies. Humans naturally enjoy well-formed patterns and melodies.
3
Understand how meters fit in
Meters (أوزان) were found to be suitable for melodies, and thus the soul inclines toward them. The combination of these two causes gives rise to poetry as a distinctive art.
This explains why poetry is metrical: it aligns with the natural love for harmonious patterns.
4
Recognize the role of natural disposition
The author notes that poetry generates 'يسيرًا يسيرًا تابعًا للطباع' – gradually, following natural dispositions. Pure individuals incline toward noble imitation, while base individuals incline toward satire.
This shows that the content of poetry reflects the poet's character and habits.

في النفس تأثيرا لا يرتاب به. ولكل غرض لحن يليق به بحسب جزالة أو لينة أو بتوسطه. وبذلك التأثير تصير النفس محاكية في نفسها لحزن أو غضب أو غير ذلك، وبالكلام نفسه إذا كان مخيلا محاكيًا، وبالوزن؛ فإن من الأوزان ما يطلس ومنها ما يوقر. وربَّما اجتمعت هذه كلها، وربَّما انفرد الوزن والكلام المخيل؛ فإن هذه الأشياء قد يفترق بعضها من بعض. وذلك أن اللحن المركب من نغم متفقة ومن إيقاع قد يوجد في المعارف والمزاهير واللحن المفرد الذي لا إيقاع فيه قد يوجد في المزامير المرسلة التي لا يوقع عليها الأصابع إذا استويت منسابة والإيقاع الذي لا لحن فيه قد يوجد في الرقص؛ ولذلك فإن الرقص مشكل جدًّا بمقارنته اللحن إياه حتى يؤثر في النفس قد تكون أقاويل منثورة مخيلة، وقد تكون أوزان غير مخيلة لأنها ساذجة لا قول. وإنما يجوز الشعر بأن يجتمع فيه القول المخيل والوزن فإن الأقاويل الموزونة التي عملها عدة من الفلاسة ومنهم سقراط قد وزنــت إما بوزن المؤلف من أربعة عشر رجلا، وإما بوزن المؤلف من ستة عشر رجلا وغير ذلك. فإن كان مؤلّف من أربعة وعشرين رجلًا وهي المقاطع. وبالجملة فالأشعار اليونانية إنما كان يقصد فيها في أكثر الأمر محاكاة الأفعال والأحوال بخلاف العرب؛ فإن شعرهم إما / لأن يؤثر في النفس كما مر، وإما للتعجب [٣٦٤] فقط. فاليونانيون كانوا يقصدون أن يحبّوا بالقول على فعل أو يردعوا بالقول عن فعل. وكانوا يفعلون ذلك تارة على سبيل الحكاية، وتارة على سبيل الشعر؛ فلذلك كانت المحاكاة عندهم مقصورة على الأفاعيل والأقوال. ثم المحاكاة مشتملة على هذه الثلاثة: [1] تشبيه، [ب] وتركيب، [ت] واستعارة؛ والأغراض ثلاثة كذلك [1] تحسين [ب] وتقبيح [ت] ومطابقة. والمطابقة من جملة ما يمكن أن يمال به إلى حسن، وأن يمال به إلى قبح كمن شبَّه شرف النفس الغضبية بوثب الأسد فإنه مطابقة يمكن أن يمال إلى الجانبين؛ فإنه يقال بوثب الأسد الضاري، أو بوثب الأسد المقدام. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن السبب المولد للشعر شيئان: [1] أحدهما الالتذاذ بالمحاكاة واستعمالها منذ الصبى؛ وللمحاكاة التي في الناس فائدة وذلك في الإشارة التي يحاكي بها المعاني فتقوم مقام التعليم، ويقع موقع سائر الأمور المتقدمة على التعليم أو حتى أن الإشارة إذا قربت بالعبارة أوقعت المعنى في النفس إيقاعا جليًّا. [ب] والثاني في حبّ الناس للتأليف المتفق والألحان طبعا. ثم قد وجدت الأوزان مناسبة للألحان فمالت إليها الأنفس وأوجد بها. فمن هذين الشيئين تولدت الشعرية وجعلت تنحو يسيرًا يسيرًا تابعة للطباع، وأكثر تولدها عن المطبوعين الذين يرتحلون الشعر طبعا انبعثت الشعرية منهم بحسب غريزة كل واحد منهم وقريحته وبحسب خلقه وعادته. فمن كان منهم أعفٌ مال إلى المحاكاة بالأفعال الجميلة أو بما يشاكله، ومن كان منهم أخس مال إلى الهجاء أو نحو ذلك من الرزائل. والمتقدمون لما تعرضوا للشعر على أوزان مختلفة كما سبق ذكرها من قبل فقد ينشأ من عمل ساطوري وهي الرباعيات من نوع وزن تذكر فيها المشهورات، والأمثال أوزان الرباعيات المشهورة بين الأمة وهي الأوزان المأخوذة من نوعي المجز أعني الأمرت والأحزم اثنا عشر من هذا النوع واثنا عشر من ذلك؛ والجملة أربعة وعشرون ليس إلا؛ وقد بينت المجموع في الرسالة المنسوبة إلى هذا الفن، ورسمتُ الكل في دائرة على سبيل الاختصار. وقد قيل في تسمية هذا النوع ساطوري إن الطبيعة صادقة ملائم الرقص المسمى ساطوريقا؛ فلهذا يسمى بهذا الاسم.
It has an effect in the soul that is not doubted. For every purpose there is a tone suitable to it according to grandeur or softness or moderation. And by that effect the soul becomes imitating in itself sadness or anger or other than that, and by the speech itself if it is imaginative and imitative, and by meter; for among meters are those that make cheerful and those that make solemn. And perhaps all these are combined, and perhaps the meter and the imaginative speech are alone; for these things may separate from one another. That is because the compound melody of consistent notes and rhythm may be found in melodies and songs, and the simple melody without rhythm may be found in the untrammeled pipes on which fingers are not placed when they are flowing evenly, and rhythm without melody may be found in dance; therefore dance is very problematic by comparing the melody to it until it affects the soul. There may be prose speeches that are imaginative, and there may be meters that are not imaginative because they are bare without speech. And poetry is valid only when imaginative speech and meter are combined together. For the metrical speeches composed by several philosophers including Socrates were weighed either by a meter composed of fourteen feet, or by a meter composed of sixteen feet, etc. If it is composed of twenty-four feet, those are the verses. In general, Greek poetry intended for the most part imitation of actions and states, contrary to the Arabs; for their poetry is either to affect the soul as mentioned, or only for wonder [364]. So the Greeks intended to incite by speech to an action or to deter by speech from an action. And they did that sometimes by way of narrative, and sometimes by way of poetry; therefore imitation with them was confined to actions and sayings. Then imitation includes these three: [1] analogy, [b] composition, [t] and metaphor; and the purposes are likewise three: [1] beautification, [b] debasement, [t] and matching. And matching is among that which can be inclined to good or can be inclined to evil, like one who likens the honor of the irascible soul to the leap of the lion, for it is a match that can be inclined to both sides; for it is said 'the leap of the predatory lion' or 'the leap of the brave lion'. If you know this, we say: The generative cause of poetry is two things: [1] One is delight in imitation and its use since childhood; and imitation has a benefit in people, namely in the indication by which meanings are imitated so that it takes the place of instruction, and it occurs in the place of other things prior to instruction or so that indication, when brought close by expression, places the meaning in the soul with a clear impact. [b] The second is people's natural love for harmonious composition and melodies. Then meters were found suitable for melodies, so the souls inclined to them and were affected by them. From these two things poetry was generated and it began to proceed little by little following natural dispositions. Most of its generation comes from naturally gifted people who spontaneously emit poetry; poetry emanated from them according to the instinct and talent of each one, and according to his nature and habit. He among them who was pure inclined to imitation of good actions or what resembles them, and he who was base inclined to satire or similar vices. The ancients, when they undertook poetry on different meters as previously mentioned, there might arise from the work of satory, which are quatrains of a type of meter in which commonplaces and proverbs are mentioned. The meters of the famous quatrains among the people are the meters taken from two types of the partial meters, namely al-amart and al-ahzam: twelve from this type and twelve from that; the total is twenty-four only. And I have explained the sum in the epistle attributed to this art, and I have drawn everything in a circle by way of abbreviation. And it was said regarding the naming of this type 'satory' that nature is truthful in agreeing with the dance called satorika; therefore it is named by this name.

The Six Constituent Parts of Tragedy
Author enumerates the six parts of tragedy with descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six parts of tragedy as defined in the passage and explain the specific function of each part in achieving tragic catharsis.

Core Idea
Tragedy's six parts build upon each other, with plot as the foundation.

This passage from an Arabic adaptation of Aristotelian poetics enumerates the essential parts of tragedy. Each part has a distinct role in moving the soul and producing emotional catharsis. By studying this enumeration, you will understand how artistic compositions are analyzed in the Islamic rational tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first part: plot/myth (خرافة)
Find where the author states that the origin and principle is 'الخرافة'. Note that it contains stories that move the soul and is the foundation of the tragedy.
This is the primary part; all others serve it.
2
Identify the second part: character (دلالة)
Look for 'الدلالة' which concerns the depiction of moral states aiming at beautification. It is about making characters appear beautiful or virtuous.
The author notes ancients excelled in this more than meter.
3
Identify the third part: thought (رأي)
Find 'الرأي' – the intellectual content expressed in speech that imitates reality and establishes beliefs through imaginative representation.
This part is about the cognitive impact, not just emotion.
4
Identify the fourth part: diction (مقالة)
Locate 'المقالة' – the verbal expression with appropriate meter and rhythm. The author discusses how changes in meter suit different purposes.
This is the linguistic form of the tragedy.
5
Identify the fifth part: melody (تلحين)
Find 'التلحين' – the musical element. The author declares it is the greatest and most effective part in influencing the soul.
Consider why music might be so powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل تركيبا ما من هذه الأشياء لا يؤدّي إلى الخصيَّة الكاملة لطراغوديا. فإن المعمول فربَّما كانت فيها خرافات واقعة؛ وكان سائر ما يقوم بها الطراغوديا موجودًا فيه، وكان يؤثر أثرًا قويا في النفس وهو قريب من الذي يسمى في زماننا مطائفة ولكنه كان يستعمل في طراغوديا بهم في أن ينتقلوا من حالة غير جميلة إلى حالة جميلة بالتدريج. والجزء الثاني الدلالة وهو أن تُقصد الحالة الجميلة بالتحسين لا من جهة تقبيح مقابلها، وكان القدماء من شعرائهم على هذا أقدر منهم على الوزن واللحن، وكان المتقدمون على إجادة الوزن واللحن أقدر منهم على حسن التمثيل بنوعي الخرافة؛ فالأصل والمبدأ هو الخرافة. ثم من بعده استعمالها في العادات على أن يقع مقارناً من الآخر حتى تحسن به المحاكاة فإن المحاكاة وهي المفرحة. والثالث من الأجزاء هو الرأي فإنَّ الرأي أبعد من العادات في التخييل؛ لأن التخييل معد نحو قبض النفس وبسطه؛ وذلك نحو ما يشتاق أن يفعل في أكثر الأمر. وكان الكلام الرأي المحمود عندهم ما اقتدر فيه على محاكاة الرأي وهو القول المطابق للموجود على أحسن ما يكون. وبالجملة فإن القدماء إنما كانوا يقررون الاعتقادات في النفوس بالتخييل الشعري. ثم يبعث الخطابة بعد ذلك فزاولوا تقرير الاعتقادات في النفوس بالإقناع؛ وكلاهما متعلق بالقول والقول في الرأي يخالف القول في العادة والخلق فإن أحدهما بحث على الإرادة، والآخر بحث على رأي في أن شيئًا موجودًا وغير موجود. والرابع المقالة وهو أن يجعل لتعرَّض المفسّرون بالقول به، ويكون ذلك الوزن مناسبا إياه، وأن تكون التغيرات الجزئية بذلك الوزن يليق به قرب شيء واحد يليق به الطنيني في غرض، وفي غرض آخر يليق به التلصيق؛ وهما فعلان متعلقان بالإيقاع. وبعد الرابعة التلحين وهو أعظم كل شيء وأشد تأثيرا في النفس. وأما النظر والاحتجاج فهو الذي تقرير في النفس حال القبول وجوب قبوله حتى يتسلى عن الغم، وينفعل الانفعال المقصود بطراغوديا. واعلم أن أصول التخيلات مأخوذة من الخطابة على أنها تقع للتصديقات، وتوابع ثم التصرف فيها بحسب أنه أصل هو الشعر وخصوصًا الطراغوديا.
But a certain composition of these things does not lead to the complete essence of tragedy. For the product may contain actual myths; and all other things that constitute tragedy are present in it, and it has a strong effect on the soul, and it is close to what is called in our time 'melodrama' but it was used in tragedy by them to transition from an unbeautiful state to a beautiful state gradually. The second part is 'indication' (character), which is to intend the beautiful state by beautification, not by way of making its opposite ugly; and the ancients among their poets were more capable in this than in meter and melody, and the earlier ones were more capable in mastering meter and melody than in good representation by the two types of myth; so the origin and principle is the myth. Then after it, its use in customs so that it occurs in conjunction with the other until imitation improves, for imitation is what gives joy. The third part is 'thought', for thought is further from customs in imaginative representation; because imaginative representation is directed toward the contraction and expansion of the soul; and that is similar to what one desires to do in most cases. And among them, praiseworthy speech in thought is that which is able to imitate thought, which is speech that corresponds to existence in the best way possible. In general, the ancients used to establish beliefs in souls through poetic imagination. Then oratory followed that, so they practiced establishing beliefs in souls through persuasion; both are related to speech, and speech in thought differs from speech in custom and character, for one is a search for will, and the other is a search for an opinion that something exists or does not exist. The fourth part is 'speech' (diction), which is to expose the interpreters by speaking through it, and that the meter be appropriate to it, and that the particular changes by that meter suit the proximity of one thing that suits the humming in one purpose, and in another purpose it suits the adhesion; and they are two actions related to rhythm. After the fourth, 'melody', which is the greatest of all things and the most effective in the soul. As for 'spectacle' and 'argument', it is that which establishes in the soul the state of acceptance and the necessity of its acceptance until one is consoled from grief, and the intended emotion is produced by tragedy. Know that the principles of imaginative representations are taken from oratory as they occur for assents, and then their adaptation according to what is the origin, which is poetry, especially tragedy.
Source pages p. 650

Commentary
This passage defines the constituent parts of tragedy within the context of Aristotelian poetics as adapted in Islamic logic. The author enumerates six parts: first, myth/plot (خرافة) – the foundational element, containing stories that move the soul. Second, character (دلالة) – the depiction of moral states aimed at beautification. Third, thought (رأي) – intellectual content expressed through speech that imitates reality. Fourth, diction (مقالة) – the verbal expression with appropriate meter and rhythm. Fifth, melody (تلحين) – the musical element, considered the most powerful in affecting the soul. Sixth, spectacle and argument (النظر والاحتجاج) – the visual presentation and logical demonstration that compel acceptance and emotional catharsis. The author emphasizes that these parts are derived from rhetoric (خطابة) and serve to produce assent (تصديق) through imaginative representation (تخييل). The passage follows the order of Aristotle's Poetics, treating each part in sequence and explaining its specific function.

Key Terms
خرافة
دلالة
رأي
مقالة
تلحين
نظر واحتجاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) خرافة (plot)
B) دلالة (character)
C) رأي (thought)
D) تلحين (melody)

Key Takeaway
The origin and principle of tragedy is the myth/plot (خرافة); all other parts serve to enhance its effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Structure of Tragedy: Beginning, Middle, and End
Author prescribes the proper composition, parts, and unity of action in tragedy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential parts of tragedy (beginning, middle, end) and state the requirements for a well-composed tragedy according to the logical tradition.

Core Idea
A well-composed tragedy requires a beginning, a middle (largest in magnitude), and an end, with natural composition and unity of action.

In the Islamic logical tradition, the art of poetry is analyzed with precision. This passage, drawn from a commentary on Aristotle's Poetics, lays down the structural rules for tragedy. Just as a syllogism must have proper premises, a tragedy must have a proper arrangement of its parts. Let us explore how the logicians defined the ideal composition of a tragedy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three structural parts of tragedy
The author states that every whole has a beginning, a middle, and an end. Locate these terms in the Arabic (مبدأ, وسط, آخر). Note that these are not just temporal but also magnitude-related.
The beginning is 'before' without needing to be 'with' something; the end is 'with' without needing to be 'before'; the middle is both 'with' and 'before' and is the separating part.
2
Understand the special status of the middle
The middle is not just intermediate in order but must be the largest in magnitude and easiest to perceive. The author emphasizes that the middle is the 'separating part' (الجزء الفاصل) and is most accessible.
3
Extract the requirements for composition
The passage requires that the composite of parts be balanced in length and width, observe natural composition, and maintain a single pattern of action (unity). This is the differentia that distinguishes a well-arranged tragedy from a poorly composed one.
The text says: 'من اللوازم أن يكون المركب من الأجزاء معتدلا في الطول والعرض' and 'أن يُراعى التركيب الطبيعي' and 'نمطا واحدا من الفعل'.
4
Formulate the definition of a well-composed tragedy
Combine the genus (imitative imaginative speech) with the differentiae (three-part structure with middle magnitude, balance, natural composition, unity of action) to form a complete description.
This is a descriptive definition (rasm), not a strict logical hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في حسن ترتيب الشعر وخصوصًا الطراغوديا وفي أجزاء الكلام المخيل الخرافي له. وأما حسن قوام الأمور التي يجب أن توجد في الأشعار فينبغي أن نتكلم فيه؛ فإن ذلك مقدمة طراغوديا، وأحكم منه وأعلى رتبة. وأيضًا فإن طراغوديا يجب أن تكون كاملة فيما يعمل من المحاكاة، وأن يعظم الأمر الذي يقصده؛ فإن تلك المعاني قد تقال قولا مرسلًا من غير الرويق والفخامة والخشمة باستعمال طراغوديا إذن بسبب التعظيم والتكميل للتخيل. وكل تمام فله مبدأ ووسط وآخر هو المنتهى والوسط مع وقبل، والمبدأ قبل؛ وليس يجب أن يكون مع والآخر مع؛ وليس يجب أن يكون قبل والجزء الفاصل هو الوسط؛ فإن كان من جهة المرتبة قد يكون بعد وليس الوسط فأصلا في المرتبة فقط بل يجب أن يكون وسطا في العظم. فإن المقدار الفاصل هو الوسط في العظم فيجب أن تكون أجزاء طراغوديا هي المتوسطة في العظم؛ وإنه أي المتوسط سهل الإدراك السهل الروية. وبالجملة فإن من اللوازم أن يكون المركب من الأجزاء معتدلا في الطول والعرض بل فيما يكون له من المقادير سواء كان ذلك المركَّب مركَّبًا من الألفاظ أو مركبًا من المعاني المدلول عليها بالألفاظ. ويجب أن يُراعى التركيب الطبيعي وإلا لا يظهر فيه الحسن، وأن يُراعى أيضًا نمطا واحدا من الفعل، ويتكلم فيه ولا يخلط أفعالاً بأفعال وأحوالاً بأحوال.
Chapter: On the proper arrangement of poetry and especially tragedy, and on the parts of imaginative speech belonging to its plot. As for the good constitution of the matters that must exist in poems, we should speak about it; for that is the introduction of tragedy, and it is more profound and of higher rank. Also, tragedy must be complete in what it produces of imitation, and must magnify the matter it aims at; for those meanings may be said in plain speech without grandeur and magnificence and dignity, but by using tragedy they are due to the magnification and completion of imagination. And every whole has a beginning, a middle, and an end that is the termination, and the middle is with and before, and the beginning is before; it is not necessary that it be with, and the end is with; it is not necessary that it be before, and the separating part is the middle; for if in terms of rank it may be after, and the middle is not fundamental in rank only, but it must be middle in magnitude. For the separating quantity is the middle in magnitude, so the parts of tragedy must be the middle in magnitude; and the middle is easy to perceive and easy to see. In general, it is necessary that the composite of parts be balanced in length and width, and in the quantities it has, whether that composite is composed of words or composed of meanings indicated by words. And the natural composition must be observed, otherwise beauty does not appear in it, and also one single pattern of action must be observed, and one speaks in it and does not mix actions with actions and states with states.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage provides rules for the proper arrangement of tragedy, part of the poetic art in logic. The author states that good composition is more important than the individual parts. Tragedy must achieve completeness and grandeur in its imitation. Every whole has three parts: beginning, middle, and end. The middle is distinguished not only by order but by magnitude; it should be the largest part and the easiest to perceive because it is the central focus. The composite of parts must be balanced in length and width, whether verbal or conceptual. Two key principles are emphasized: natural composition (التركيب الطبيعي) without which beauty is absent, and unity of action (نمطا واحدا من الفعل) – the tragedy should focus on a single type of action and not mix different actions or states. This echoes Aristotle's unity of plot.

وسط

Key Takeaway
An ideal tragedy must be composed of three parts (beginning, middle, end) with the middle as the largest and easiest part, balanced in proportion, natural in order, and unified in action.

Reasoning and Inclusion in Poetic Imagination
Author defines roles of reasoning and inclusion in poetic imagination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between reasoning (istidlāl) and inclusion (ishtimāl) in poetic fiction and explain how they combine to affect the soul.

Core Idea
The most powerful poetry combines reasoning with inclusion to move the soul.

This passage from a classical logic text explores how poetry and rhetoric move the soul. It introduces two key components of poetic fiction: reasoning (istidlāl) and inclusion (ishtimāl). Understanding their interplay is essential for analysing how Islamic scholars used poetic devices in da'wa, Qur'anic interpretation, and theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core terms
Locate the terms 'istidlāl' (reasoning) and 'ishtimāl' (inclusion) in the Arabic. Note that reasoning here is not logical demonstration but an emotional-cognitive process that transfers the soul from one state to another.
2
Analyse their relationship
The author states that the best reasoning is composed with inclusion. What does this mean? Reasoning provides a structure, while inclusion adds emotional texture. Imagine a poem that argues a point (reasoning) and uses vivid imagery (inclusion) to make it feel real.
3
Understand the effect on the soul
The passage says that imagining happiness causes expansion (inbisāt) and imagining misery causes contraction (inqibād). Reflect on how a well-crafted line of Arabic poetry can make you feel joy or fear, as in the imitation of great calamities like a flood.
This emotional transfer is central to the art of tragedy (trāghūdiyā).
4
Identify the application in tragedy
Tragedy uses reasoning and inclusion to evoke tenderness or fear. The author adds that tragedy also includes amplification (tahwīl) and intensification of emotion. Consider how the Qur'anic stories of past nations use such devices to warn and move the listener.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجرّبات وذلك أكثره في الجزء الذاتي. وكثير من الخرافات قد تكون خالية عن النفع في التخييل؛ وربَّما كان بعضها مشتبكا متداخلا به يحجج كما أن الأفعال من الناس بعضها ينال به الغرض لبساطة وبكونه واحدا متصلا، وبعضها إنما يقال به الغرض تركيب وتخليط والمشتبك ما كان منتفيًا في وجوه الاستدلال والاشتمال. وبذلك تنقل النفس من حال إلى حال وإن كان اشتمال و استدلال یراد به فعل النفس إلى انفعال عن انفعال بأن تخيل سعادة فتنبسط، أو شقاوة فتنقبض. وأحسن الاستدلال ما يتركب بالاشتمال؛ وقد يستعمل الاستدلال في كل شيء، وتكون منه خرافة لكن الأليق بهذا الموضع أن يكون الاستدلال على فعل. فإن مثل هذا الاستدلال وما يجري مجراه من الاشتمال هو الذي يؤثر في النفس رقّة أو مخافة كما يحتاج إليه في طراغوديا. ولأن التحسين وإظهار السعادة، والتقبيح وإظهار الشقاوة إنما يتعلق في ظاهر المشهور بالأفعال فأجزاء الخرافة في الأول الاستدلال والاشتمال. وهنا أجزاء يتبعها في طراغوديا وهو التهويل وتعظيم الأمر، وتشديد الانفعال مثل ما يعرض عند محاكاة الآفات الشاملة نحو الطوفان وغير ذلك.
The things experienced, and most of that is in the essential part. Many fictions may be free from benefit in imaginative representation; and perhaps some of them are intertwined and intermingled with it such that they argue, just as the actions of some people achieve the purpose by simplicity and by being one continuous thing, and some [actions] achieve the purpose by composition and mixture. The intertwined is that which is removed in the aspects of reasoning and inclusion. And by that the soul is transferred from one state to another, although inclusion and reasoning are intended as the action of the soul towards an emotion from an emotion, such that it imagines happiness and expands, or misery and contracts. The best reasoning is that which is composed with inclusion; reasoning may be used in everything, and from it comes fiction, but the most appropriate in this context is that reasoning be upon action. For such reasoning and what runs in its course from inclusion is what affects the soul with tenderness or fear, as needed in tragedy. And because beautification and showing happiness, and uglification and showing misery, are only concerned, in apparent common opinion, with actions, so the parts of fiction in the first are reasoning and inclusion. And here are parts that follow in tragedy, which are the amplification and magnification of the matter, and intensification of emotion, like what occurs in the imitation of comprehensive calamities like the flood and others.

Commentary
This passage defines the concepts of reasoning (استدلال) and inclusion (اشتمال) in the context of poetic fiction (خرافة) and their effect on the soul. The author states that most empirical matters are in the essential part, while many fictions lack benefit in imaginative representation; some fictions are intertwined and produce argumentation, similar to how human actions achieve their purpose either through simplicity or through composition. The intertwined (مشتبك) is defined as that which lacks reasoning and inclusion. Reasoning and inclusion transfer the soul from state to state: imagining happiness causes expansion (انبساط), imagining misery causes contraction (انقباض). The best reasoning is that composed with inclusion. Reasoning can be used for anything, producing fiction, but here it is most appropriate for action. Such reasoning and inclusion affect the soul with tenderness or fear, as needed in tragedy (طراغوديا). The author explains that beautification and uglification relate to actions in popular opinion, so the parts of fiction are reasoning and inclusion, and in tragedy there are additional parts like amplification (تهويل) and intensification of emotion, as when imitating great calamities like a flood. This passage lays the foundation for understanding how poetic devices work to influence the audience's emotions, a key element of the art of rhetoric and poetics in the Islamic logical tradition.

The Four Parts of Tragedy: Definitions and Order
Author classifies the parts of tragedy according to order and recitation: entrance, exit, passage, and evaluation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four structural parts of tragedy according to order and recitation, and define each part's characteristics.

Core Idea
Tragedy has four ordered parts defined by performance method: entrance, exit, passage, and evaluation.

In the Islamic logical tradition, poetics ('ilm al-shi'r) is one of the five arts. This passage from Mantiq Kabir classifies the parts of tragedy (ancient Greek drama) according to how they are arranged and performed. Understanding this structure helps us see how logical classification applies to artistic composition, a skill transferable to analyzing Islamic poetry and oratory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four parts listed
Scan the Arabic passage for the four named parts: المدخل, المخرج, المجاز, and التقويم. Note their order: entrance, then exit, then passage, then evaluation. The passage also notes that entrance and passage are always present.
Be aware that 'majāz' here means passage, not metaphor.
2
Extract the defining characteristic of each part
For each part, find the sentence that explains how it is performed. Entrance: universal part containing sub-parts, melodists begin in its middle. Exit: part after which the group stops singing. Passage: performed without melody but with rhythm. Evaluation: performed without rhythm, with chanting using only poetic meter.
The performance methods are key: melody, rhythm, chanting.
3
Analyze the analogy to Arab poetry and oratory
The author compares the entrance, exit, and passage to the preface (صدر) of a speech, and the evaluation to narration and confirmation (اقتصاص وتصديق) in oratory. This shows how the classification is rooted in familiar Arabic rhetorical patterns.
The analogy helps memorize the order and function of each part.
4
Check the completeness of each definition
The entrance is called 'universal part containing parts' – this suggests it is a genus with species. The others are defined by what happens after them (exit) or by performance method (passage, evaluation). Consider if these definitions are complete (hadd) or merely descriptive (rasm).
The passage does not explicitly state whether these are hadd or rasm, but the structure invites analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في أجزاء طراغوديا بحسب الترتيب والإنشاد لا بحسب المعاني قد كان عندهم لكل قصيدة من طراغوديا أجزاء تترتب عليه في ابتدائها ووسطها وانتهائها؛ وكان ينشد بالغناء الرقصي؛ ويتولاه عدة، وكان جزؤه الذي يقوم مقام الأول في شعر العرب يسمى مدخلا؛ ثم يليه جزء يبتدئ معه الرقاص يسمّى مخرج الرقاص. ثمَّ جزء آخر يسمى مجاز هؤلاء؛ وهذا كله كالصدر في الخطبة، ثم يشرعون فيما يجري مجرى الاقتصاص والتصديق في الخطابة يسمَّى التقويم، ثمَّ كانت تختلف أحوال ذلك في مساكنهم وبلادهم وإن كان لا يخلو عن المدخل والمجاز؛ فالمدخل هو جزء كلي مشتمل على أجزاء، وفي وسطه يبتدئ الملحنون بجماعتهم. والمخرج هو الجزء الذي لا يلحن بعده الجماعة منهم. وأما المجاز فهو الذي يؤدى بلا لحن بل بإيقاع، وأما التقويم فهو الجزء الذي لا يؤدى بنوع من الإيقاع يستعمل فيما سواه بل يؤدى بتنشيد لا عمل معه إلا وزن الشعر ؛ وكل ذلك ينشده جماعة الملحنين فهو نوع قسمة الطراغوديا.
Section: On the parts of tragedy according to order and recitation, not according to meanings. They had for every poem of tragedy parts that are arranged in its beginning, middle, and end; it was recited with dance-song; and several people undertook it. Its part that takes the place of the first in Arab poetry is called entrance (mudkhal). Then follows a part with which the dancer begins, called the dancer's exit (mukharraj al-raqqās). Then another part called their passage (majāz). All of this is like the preface in a speech; then they proceed to what runs in the course of narration and confirmation in oratory, called evaluation (taqwīm). Then the conditions of that varied in their dwellings and lands, although it does not lack the entrance and passage. The entrance is a universal part containing parts; in its middle the melodists begin with their group. The exit is the part after which the group from among them does not sing. As for the passage, it is performed without melody but with rhythm. As for the evaluation, it is the part that is not performed with the kind of rhythm used in other parts; rather it is performed with chanting with no action except the meter of poetry. All of that is recited by the group of melodists, and it is a type of division of tragedy.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the structural parts of tragedy (طراغوديا) according to performance order and recitation method, not according to meaning. The author explains that each tragedy had parts arranged in beginning, middle, and end. It was performed with dance-song (غناء رقصي) by multiple performers. The first part, analogous to the beginning in Arab poetry, is called the entrance (مدخل). The next part, with which the dancer begins, is called the dancer's exit (مخرج الرقاص). Another part is called their passage (مجاز). These function like the preface (صدر) in a speech. Then they proceed to what is like narration and confirmation in oratory, called evaluation (تقويم). The author notes variations by region, but entrance and passage are always present. Each part is defined: the entrance is a universal part containing sub-parts, in the middle of which the melodists begin; the exit is the part after which the group stops singing; the passage is performed without melody but with rhythm; the evaluation is performed without rhythm but with chanting using only poetic meter. All parts are recited by the group of melodists, constituting a division of tragedy. This classification is important for understanding how logic (specifically poetics) analyzes the structure of persuasive and emotional discourse.

Key Terms
المدخل
المخرج
المجاز
التقويم
الإنشاد

Key Takeaway
Tragedy is divided into four ordered parts — entrance, exit, passage, and evaluation — each defined by its performance method (melody, rhythm, chanting) and position in the sequence.
